Divorce of Non-Consummated Wife and Non-Menstruating Women
Rulings on divorcing non-consummated and non-menstruating women with Zufar's ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorcing a non-consummated wife and a non-menstruating woman, and explain Zufar's opposing position.

Core Idea
Non-consummated wives and non-menstruating women have no 'iddah, so divorce is permissible anytime without waiting periods.

This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira addresses two exceptions to the general sunnah timing of divorce: the non-consummated wife and the woman who does not menstruate. The Hanafi school permits immediate divorce in both cases, but Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl disagrees, requiring a waiting period. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your grasp of the rationale behind the rules of talaq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases and each Hanafi ruling.
The passage gives two scenarios: a non-consummated wife (ghayr al-madkhūl bihā) and a woman who does not menstruate due to youth or old age. State the Hanafi permissibility in each case: the non-consummated wife may be divorced in purity or menstruation; the non-menstruating woman may be divorced immediately after intercourse.
2
Locate Zufar's ikhtilaf for each case.
Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl opposes on both points: he prohibits divorce during menstruation for the non-consummated wife, and requires a one-month wait after intercourse before the first divorce for the non-menstruating woman.
Zufar's views are recorded as nawadir (rare transmissions).
3
Trace the Hanafi reasoning for both.
The Hanafi view relies on the absence of 'iddah and the fear of pregnancy. For the non-consummated wife, no 'iddah exists; for the non-menstruating woman, there is no possibility of pregnancy or prolonged 'iddah. Therefore, the restrictions that apply to a menstruating consummated wife do not apply.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf.
The practical difference: under the Hanafi view, a non-consummated wife can be divorced during menstruation; under Zufar's view, she cannot. For a non-menstruating woman, the Hanafi view allows immediate divorce after intercourse; Zufar requires a one-month delay.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi.
Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira presents Al-Quduri's positions without challenge, indicating they are the authoritative, relied-upon views within the Hanafi school. Zufar's opinions are not followed for fatwa.
The mufta bihi is: immediate divorce permissible in both cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق
وغير المدخول بها أن يطلقها في حال الطهر والحيض جميعاً . وإذا كانت المرأةُ ممن لا تحيض من صِغَرٍ أو كبر، فأراد أن يطلقها للسنة : طلقها واحدةً، فإذا مضى شهر : طلَّقها أخرى، فإذا مضى شهر آخر : طلقها أخرى.
ويجوز أن يطلقها ولا يفصل بين وطئها وطلاقها بزمان .
على طلاقها، وهذا لا يُتصور إلا في المدخولة.
وأما غير المدخولة : فلا تثبت فيها السنَّةُ في الوقت، حتى إنه لا يكره طلاقها وهي حائض؛ لأنها لا عدة عليها. قوله: (وغير المدخول بها : يطلقها في حالة الطهر والحيض).
وقال زفر : لا يطلقها في حالة الحيض.
* قوله : (وإذا كانت المرأةُ ممن لا تحيض من صِغَر أو كبر، وأراد أن يُطلقها للسنة: طلقها واحدةً متى شاء؛ لأن المانع من طلاق الحائض تطويل العدة، وخوفُ الحَبَل، وهذا معدوم في الآيسة، والصغيرة.
وقال زفر : لا يطلقها حتى يمضي شهر بعد ما جامعها.
فإن أراد أن يحصل لها طلاق السنة بالعدد: طلقها واحدةً متى شاء، (ثم يتركها حتى يمضي شهر ، ثم يطلقها أخرى ، ثم يتركها شهراً، ثم يطلقها أخرى). * قوله: (ويجوز أن يطلقها ولا يفصل بين وطئها وطلاقها بزمان).
يعني التي لا تحيض من صِغَرٍ أو كبر.
Book of Divorce. And the non-consummated wife is divorced in both purity and menstruation. And if the woman is among those who do not menstruate due to youth or old age, and he wants to divorce her according to the sunnah: he divorces her once, then when a month passes, he divorces her again, then when another month passes, he divorces her again. And it is permissible for him to divorce her without separating between intercourse and divorce by any time. Upon her divorce, this is only conceivable for a consummated wife. As for the non-consummated one, the sunnah is not established in time, such that it is not disliked to divorce her while she is menstruating because she has no waiting period. He said: (And the non-consummated wife: he divorces her in the state of purity and menstruation). And Zufar said: He should not divorce her in the state of menstruation. * He said: (And if the woman is among those who do not menstruate due to youth or old age, and he wants to divorce her according to the sunnah: he divorces her once whenever he wishes; because the prohibition of divorcing a menstruating woman is due to prolonging the waiting period and fear of pregnancy, and this is absent in the elderly and young). And Zufar said: He should not divorce her until a month passes after he has intercourse with her. If he wants to obtain for her a sunnah divorce in number: he divorces her once whenever he wishes, (then he leaves her until a month passes, then he divorces her again, then he leaves her for a month, then he divorces her again). * He said: (And it is permissible for him to divorce her without separating between intercourse and divorce by any time). Meaning the one who does not menstruate due to youth or old age.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira, a commentary on Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, deals with two related rulings on divorce timing. First, for a non-consummated wife (غير المدخول بها), the husband may divorce her in either a state of purity or menstruation without any prohibition, because she has no 'iddah (waiting period). The text emphasizes that the sunnah regarding time does not apply to her, so it is not even disliked (makruh) to divorce her while menstruating. Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl, a prominent Hanafi scholar, disagrees and holds that divorcing her during menstruation is not permissible. Second, for women who do not menstruate due to young age (صغيرة) or old age (آيسة), the husband may divorce her immediately after intercourse without waiting. However, if he intends to give three divorces according to the sunnah, he should give one divorce, wait a month, give another, wait another month, and then give the third. Zufar here requires a one-month delay after intercourse before the first divorce for such women. The Hanafi position is based on the absence of the reasons that prohibit divorcing a menstruating woman—namely, lengthening the 'iddah and fear of pregnancy. For the non-menstruating woman, these concerns do not exist, so immediate divorce is permissible. Zufar's view is considered less strong and not followed in practice.

Key Terms
غير المدخول بها (ghayr al-madkhūl bihā): A wife with whom the husband has not consummated the marriage.
آيسة (ā'isa): A woman who has reached menopause and no longer menstruates.
صغيرة (saghīra): A young girl who has not yet reached the age of menstruation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only in a state of purity
B) Only in a state of menstruation
C) In both purity and menstruation
D) Only after a month has passed since the last intercourse

Key Takeaway
The absence of 'iddah for non-consummated and non-menstruating women removes the restrictions on divorce timing, making immediate divorce permissible.

Divorce of a Pregnant Woman and Divorce During Menstruation
Ruling on divorce of pregnant woman and during menstruation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorcing a pregnant woman (number of divorces and interval) and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad/Zufar.

Core Idea
Divorcing a pregnant woman is permissible immediately; the sunnah method is three divorces with monthly intervals according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

This lesson addresses two specific cases of divorce: the pregnant woman and the woman during menstruation. You will learn not only the ruling but also the reasoning behind the intra-Hanafi disagreement on the number of sunnah divorces for a pregnant woman, as well as the ruling for divorce during menstruation, which is valid but contrary to sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Separate the two scenarios
The passage covers two distinct cases: divorce of a pregnant woman and divorce during menstruation. Identify each and note that they have different rules.
2
State the majority Hanafi position on pregnant woman
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that sunnah divorce for a pregnant woman is three divorces with a month between each. The divorce is permissible immediately after intercourse.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Identify the minority position and its proof
Muhammad and Zufar hold that only one divorce is sunnah. Their proof: the 'iddah of a pregnant woman ends with childbirth, so the month interval is not part of her 'iddah; the basic principle of divorce is prohibition (so minimal is better).
4
Analyze the majority's response (qiyas)
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf analogize (qiyas) the pregnant woman to non-menstruating women (elderly and young) where a month interval is observed. They argue the month serves as a symbolic separation, not part of 'iddah.
This is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy).
5
Determine the ruling on divorce during menstruation
Divorce during menstruation is valid (sahih) but contrary to sunnah; it is recommended (mustahab) for the husband to revoke (yuraji') the divorce.
Zufar adds that for a pregnant woman, a month should separate intercourse and divorce, but the majority does not require this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطلاق الحامل يجوز عَقِيب الجماع، ويطلقها للسنة ثلاثاً، يَفْصِلُ بين كل تطليقتين بشهر عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف.
وقال محمد : لا يطلقها للسنة إلا واحدة.
وإذا طلق الرجل امرأته في حال الحيض : وقع الطلاق.
ويُستحب له أن يراجعها،
وقال زفر : يفصل بين وطئها وطلاقها بشهر .
والخلاف فيما إذا كانت صغيرة لا يُرجى منها الحيض والحبل، أما إذا كانت يرجى منها ذلك : فالأفضل أن يفصل بين وطئها وطلاقها بشهر،
إجماعاً.
قوله: (وطلاق الحامل يجوز عَقِيب الجماع)؛ لأنه لا يؤدي إلى اشتباه العدة.
قوله: (ويطلقها للسنة ثلاثاً، يفصل بين كل تطليقتين بشهر عندهما، وقال محمد وزفر : لا يطلقها للسنة إلا واحدة). لأن الأصل في الطلاق الحظر، وقد ورد الشرع بالتفريق على فصول وهي الأشهر، أو الحيض، والشهر في حق الحامل ليس من العدة، فصولها، وهما يقيسانها على الآيسة والصغيرة.
قوله: (وإذا طلق الرجل امرأته في حال الحيض: وقع الطلاق، ويُستحب له أن يراجعها).
And the divorce of a pregnant woman is permissible immediately after intercourse, and he divorces her for the sunnah three times, separating between each two divorces by a month according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: He does not divorce her for the sunnah except once. And if a man divorces his wife during menstruation, the divorce occurs. And it is recommended for him to take her back. And Zufar said: He separates between his intercourse and her divorce by a month. And the disagreement is concerning a young girl from whom menstruation and pregnancy are not expected; but if it is expected from her, then it is better to separate between intercourse and divorce by a month, by consensus. He said: (And the divorce of a pregnant woman is permissible immediately after intercourse) because it does not lead to confusion of the waiting period. He said: (And he divorces her for the sunnah three times, separating between each two divorces by a month according to them, and Muhammad and Zufar said: He does not divorce her for the sunnah except once). Because the original ruling regarding divorce is prohibition, and the Law has come with separation into intervals, which are months or menstruations, and the month with respect to the pregnant woman is not part of her waiting period, its intervals; and they (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) analogize her to the elderly and young girl. He said: (And if a man divorces his wife during menstruation, the divorce occurs, and it is recommended for him to take her back).
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on sunnah divorce (طلاق السنة) by addressing two specific cases: the pregnant woman and the woman divorced during menstruation. First, divorcing a pregnant woman is permissible immediately after intercourse because it does not lead to confusion about the 'iddah (her 'iddah is determined by childbirth). Regarding the number of divorces for a sunnah divorce, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that he should give three divorces, with a month between each. Muhammad al-Shaybani and Zufar, however, hold that only one divorce is sunnah for a pregnant woman, reasoning that the month interval is not part of her 'iddah (which ends with childbirth) and the basic principle of divorce is prohibition (so minimal deviation is better). The commentary explains that Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf analogize (qiyas) the pregnant woman to the non-menstruating woman (آيسة or صغيرة), where a month interval is observed. Second, regarding divorce during menstruation: the divorce takes effect (يقع) and is valid, but it is recommended (يستحب) for the husband to take her back (يراجعها) because it is contrary to the sunnah. Zufar adds that for a pregnant woman, a month should separate intercourse and divorce, but the mainstream Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) does not require this for pregnant women, only for those from whom menstruation and pregnancy are expected (i.e., non-pregnant menstruating women).

Key Terms
الحامل (al-ḥāmil): A pregnant woman; her 'iddah is determined by childbirth.
السنة (al-sunnah): The recommended method of divorce, involving intervals between divorces.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) One divorce
B) Two divorces
C) Three divorces, with a month between each
D) Three divorces in one sitting

Key Takeaway
Divorce of a pregnant woman is permissible immediately after intercourse; the sunnah method is three divorces with monthly intervals according to the majority, while Muhammad and Zufar hold it is only one.

Sunnah Divorce of a Pregnant Woman: Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on sunnah divorce of pregnant woman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the permissibility of divorcing a pregnant woman immediately after intercourse and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad/Zufar regarding the number of divorces in a sunnah divorce.

Core Idea
Divorce of a pregnant woman is valid immediately; the sunnah method's number is disputed.

The sunnah (recommended) method of divorce requires specific intervals to avoid confusion about parentage. For a pregnant woman, her 'iddah ends with delivery, so divorce immediately after intercourse does not cause confusion. However, the Hanafi imams differ on how many divorces may be given in a sunnah manner to a pregnant woman. This lesson unpacks their arguments and the basis used by each side.

Guided Path
1
Understand the ruling: permissibility of immediate divorce
The matn states that divorcing a pregnant woman is permissible immediately after intercourse because her 'iddah ends with delivery, preventing confusion of parentage. This is the base ruling.
This ruling applies regardless of the sunnah method dispute.
2
Identify the dispute on the number of divorces
The text says 'he divorces her for the sunnah three times, separating between every two divorces by a month according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: He does not divorce her for the sunnah except one.' This is the core ikhtilaf.
The dispute is about the sunnah method, not the validity of the divorce itself.
3
Examine the reasoning of Muhammad and Zufar
Muhammad and Zufar argue that the origin of divorce is prohibition (al-hazr), and the Law permits it only with intervals (months or menstruation). Since a pregnant woman does not menstruate, and her 'iddah is not completed by months (but by delivery), she is like a menopausal woman or a young girl, for whom only one divorce is allowed.
They use qiyas to the menopausal and young girl.
4
Examine the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf analogize the pregnant woman to other women who have intervals by months, such as the menopausal woman. Since her 'iddah ends with delivery, there is no risk of confusion, so three divorces spaced by a month are permissible.
They consider the month as a valid interval even though it is not part of her 'iddah.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (relied-upon position)
The majority of Hanafi scholars follow the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: three divorces spaced by a month. This is the operative position for fatwa.
This tarjih is based on the strength of the analogy and the avoidance of undue restriction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطلاق الحامل يجوز عَقِيب الجماع، ويطلقها للسنة ثلاثاً، يَفْصِلُ بين كل تطليقتين بشهر عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وقال محمد : لا يطلقها للسنة إلا واحدة. قوله: (وطلاق الحامل يجوز عَقِيب الجماع)؛ لأنه لا يؤدي إلى اشتباه العدة. قوله: (ويطلقها للسنة ثلاثاً، يفصل بين كل تطليقتين بشهر عندهما، وقال محمد وزفر : لا يطلقها للسنة إلا واحدة). لأن الأصل في الطلاق الحظر، وقد ورد الشرع بالتفريق على فصول وهي الأشهر، أو الحيض، والشهر في حق الحامل ليس من العدة، فصولها، وهما يقيسانها على الآيسة والصغيرة.
And the divorce of a pregnant woman is permissible immediately after intercourse, and he divorces her for the sunnah three times, separating between every two divorces by a month according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: He does not divorce her for the sunnah except one. His statement: (And the divorce of a pregnant woman is permissible immediately after intercourse) because it does not lead to confusion of the 'iddah. His statement: (And he divorces her for the sunnah three times, separating between every two divorces by a month according to them, and Muhammad and Zufar said: He does not divorce her for the sunnah except one) because the origin regarding divorce is prohibition, and the Law has come with differentiation by intervals, which are months or menstruation, and the month with respect to the pregnant woman is not part of her 'iddah, so its intervals, and they (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) analogize her to the menopausal and the young girl.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the sunnah divorce of a pregnant woman. The matn states the ruling: it is permissible to divorce a pregnant woman immediately after intercourse, without waiting a month. The dispute is about the number of divorces allowed in a sunnah divorce for such a woman. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that he may give three divorces, each separated by a month, because the 'iddah of a pregnant woman ends with delivery, so there is no fear of confusion of the 'iddah. Muhammad and Zufar argue that only one divorce is permitted, because the sunnah divorce is based on intervals of purity or months, but a pregnant woman does not have menstruation, so the standard sunnah method does not apply; she is analogous to a menopausal woman or a young girl who does not menstruate, for whom only one divorce is allowed. The commentary explains the reasoning: the origin of divorce is prohibition (al-hazr), and the Law permits it with intervals. Since a pregnant woman's 'iddah is not completed by months (but by delivery), spacing divorces by month has no basis; thus the two imams (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) analogize her to other women, while the other two restrict it.

Key Terms
طلاق الحامل (talāq al-ḥāmil): Divorce of a pregnant woman; it is permissible immediately after intercourse because her ‘iddah ends with delivery, preventing confusion of parentage.
الطلاق للسنة (al-ṭalāq li al-sunnah): The sunnah divorce is given during a period of purity without intercourse, spaced appropriately (by menstrual cycles for non-pregnant women, by months for pregnant women according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).
عدة (‘iddah): The waiting period after divorce; for a non-pregnant woman, it is three menstrual cycles or three months; for a pregnant woman, it ends with delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi dispute, what is the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the number of divorces in a sunnah divorce for a pregnant woman?
A) Only one divorce is allowed.
B) Three divorces, each separated by a month.
C) Two divorces, separated by one menstrual cycle.
D) Three divorces, with no separation required.

Key Takeaway
The sunnah divorce of a pregnant woman is valid immediately after intercourse; the number of divorces is disputed within the Hanafi school, with the relied-upon view being three spaced by a month.

Divorce During Menstruation: Obligation of Reconciliation and Timing Dispute
Ruling on menstrual divorce and intra-Hanafi dispute on subsequent divorce timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the obligation of taking back a wife after a menstrual divorce and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on the timing of a subsequent sunnah divorce.

Core Idea
Taking back a wife after a menstrual divorce is wajib; the dispute is when a subsequent divorce is permissible.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on divorce pronounced during menstruation. While the divorce itself is valid, the husband is obliged to take his wife back immediately. The text then reveals an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Zufar on one side and the Two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) on the other regarding the timing of a subsequent sunnah divorce after reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling
State the ruling on divorce during menstruation. It is valid but considered bid'i (innovative). The initial ruling on taking her back is either recommended or obligatory.
The matn says 'mustahab', but the commentary corrects it to 'wajib'.
2
Trace the proof for obligation
Identify the dalil: the hadith of 'Umar where the Prophet (peace be upon him) commanded 'let him take her back'. In Hanafi usul, the imperative (amr) indicates obligation unless there is a contrary indicator.
The objection about the command being to 'Umar personally is answered by the principle 'the action of the deputy is like the action of the principal'.
3
Understand the dispute on subsequent divorce timing
After reconciliation, the wife must complete two menstrual cycles before a sunnah divorce can occur. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a second divorce can be pronounced in the first purity period after reconciliation.
Abu Hanifa and Zufar allow it; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad require waiting for another purity.
4
Analyze the basis of each view
The Two base their view on the need for a full cycle to ensure the wife is not pregnant. Abu Hanifa and Zufar consider the speech of taking back as resetting the clock.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text indicates that the relied-upon view for the obligation is wajib (al-asahh). For the timing dispute, it presents both views without explicit tarjih, but later practice inclines to Abu Hanifa and Zufar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وإذا طلق الرجل امرأته في حال الحيض: وقع الطلاق، ويُستحب له أن يراجعها). والخلاف فيما إذا كانت صغيرة لا يُرجى منها الحيض والحبل، أما إذا كانت يرجى منها ذلك : فالأفضل أن يفصل بين وطئها وطلاقها بشهر، إجماعاً. فإذا طهرت، وحاضت وطهرت، فهو مخيَّر : إن شاء طلقها، وإن شاء أمسكها. الاستحباب قول بعض المشايخ والأصح أنه واجب، عملاً بحقيقة الأمر، وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لعمر رضي الله عنه: من ابنك فليراجعها، وكان قد طلقها حائض (۱). وهي فإن قيل: الأمر إنما أثبت الوجوب على عمر أن يأمر ابنه بالمراجعة، فكيف يثبت وجوب المراجعة بقول عمر؟ قلنا : فعل النائب: كفعل المنوب عنه، فصار كأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم هو الذي أمره بالمراجعة، فثبت الوجوب. قال الخُجندي: والخلع في حالة الحيض مكروه، في رواية «الزيادات». وفي «المنتقى»: لا بأس به في حالة الحيض إذا رأى منها ما يكره. * قوله: (فإذا طهرت وحاضت ثم طهرت: فهو بالخيار: إن شاء طلقها، وإن شاء أمسكها، وهذا قولهما. وقال أبو حنيفة وزفر : إذا راجعها بالقول بعد ما طلقها في الحيض: جاز أن يطلقها في الطهر الذي يلي تلك الحيضة. وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا طلقها في طُهْرٍ لا جماع فيه، ثم راجعها في ذلك الطهر بالقول، وأراد أن يطلقها أخرى للسنة في ذلك الطهر : فله ذلك عند أبي حنيفة وزفر.
His statement: (And if a man divorces his wife during menstruation: the divorce occurs, and it is recommended for him to take her back.) And the disagreement regarding when she is a young girl from whom menstruation and pregnancy are not expected; as for when it is expected from her, it is better to separate between intercourse and divorce by a month, by consensus. Then when she becomes pure, and menstruates, and becomes pure, he has the choice: if he wishes he divorces her, and if he wishes he keeps her. [The word] recommendation is the saying of some shaykhs, but the correct view is that it is obligatory, acting by the reality of the command, and it is His saying, peace and blessings be upon him, to ‘Umar, may Allah be pleased with him: 'From your son, let him take her back' and he had divorced her while she was menstruating. And if it is said: The command only established the obligation on ‘Umar to order his son to take her back, so how is the obligation of taking her back established by the saying of ‘Umar? We say: The action of the deputy is like the action of the one deputized, so it is as if the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, is the one who ordered him to take her back, so obligation is established. al-Khujandi said: And khul' during menstruation is disliked, in the narration of al-Ziyadat. And in al-Muntaqa: There is no harm in it during menstruation if he sees from her what he dislikes. * His statement: (Then when she becomes pure, menstruates, then becomes pure: he has the choice: if he wishes he divorces her, and if he wishes he keeps her, and this is their saying. And Abu Hanifa and Zufar said: If he takes her back by speech after divorcing her during menstruation, it is permissible for him to divorce her in the purity that follows that menstruation. And on this ikhtilaf, if he divorces her in a purity without intercourse, then takes her back in that purity by speech, and wants to divorce her another time for the sunnah in that purity: he has that according to Abu Hanifa and Zufar.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of divorce during menstruation. The matn initially states that divorce during menstruation is valid (falls) and that it is recommended (mustaḥabb) to take her back. The commentary then refines this: some scholars say it is recommended, but the correct view (al-aṣaḥḥ) is that it is obligatory (wājib), based on the Prophet's command to ‘Umar to order his son to take back his wife whom he had divorced while she was menstruating. The proof is a ḥadīth; the objection about the command being to ‘Umar personally is answered by the principle that the action of the deputy is treated as the action of the principal. Then, a secondary ruling: after he takes her back (or without taking back, as per the sequence), she must menstruate twice; then he has the choice to either divorce her or keep her. However, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa/Zufar and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the timing of a subsequent sunnah divorce. According to Abu Hanifa and Zufar, if the husband takes her back by speech after divorcing her during menstruation, he may divorce her again in the same purity period that immediately follows that menstruation (i.e., before her next cycle). The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that he must wait until after she menstruates and becomes pure again (i.e., after two cycles). This same dispute applies when he divorces her in a purity without intercourse, then takes her back by speech in that same purity: Abu Hanifa and Zufar allow a second divorce in that same purity, while the two disagree. Additionally, the text includes a note on khul‘ during menstruation: according to one narration (from al-Ziyadat) it is disliked (makrūh), but according to al-Muntaqa, there is no harm if he sees something objectionable from her. The commentary also clarifies that the earlier ikhtilaf about small girls (who do not menstruate) is resolved by consensus that a month should separate intercourse and divorce.

Key Terms
المراجعة (al-murāja‘ah): Taking back the wife during the ‘iddah after a revocable divorce; in this passage, it is ruled wajib after a menstrual divorce.
الخلع (al-khal‘): A divorce in exchange for payment by the wife; its ruling during menstruation is disputed – some narrations say it is disliked, others permit it if there is dislike.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Obligatory (wājib).
C) Permissible (mubāḥ).
D) Forbidden (ḥarām).

Key Takeaway
Divorce during menstruation is valid but obligates the husband to immediately take back his wife; subsequent divorce timing is disputed within the school.

Timing of Subsequent Divorce After Revocation During Menstruation
Dispute on timing of divorce after revocation during menstruation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding the permissibility of divorce in the purity following a menstrual cycle after revocation by word, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Revocation by word during menstruation: Abu Hanifa allows divorce in the next purity, Abu Yusuf forbids it.

When a husband divorces his wife during her menstrual period (which is prohibited but valid), he is obligated to revoke the divorce. But if he revokes by word, when may he issue another divorce? The Hanafi masters disagreed on whether he must wait for a full menstrual cycle or may do so in the immediate purity. This lesson unpacks that ikhtilaf and its practical impact.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The matn states that after the woman becomes pure, menstruates, and becomes pure again, the husband has a choice. This is the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Understand what this means: he must wait for a full cycle after the original divorce.
2
Locate the ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa and Zufar disagree: if the husband revokes by word during the menstruation in which he divorced, he may divorce her in the purity immediately after that menstruation, without waiting for another cycle.
3
Extend the scenario
The same dispute applies if the husband divorces in a purity without intercourse, then revokes by word in that same purity. Abu Hanifa allows another divorce in that purity; Abu Yusuf does not.
4
Note the confusion on Muhammad's position
Al-Tahawi reports Muhammad agrees with Abu Hanifa; Abu al-Layth reports he agrees with Abu Yusuf. The passage does not resolve this.
5
Apply to other revocation methods
The difference also applies if revocation is by touch, kiss, or look. However, if revocation is by intercourse, all agree that the husband cannot divorce in that same purity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق

الاستحباب قول بعض المشايخ والأصح أنه واجب، عملاً بحقيقة الأمر، وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لعمر رضي الله عنه: مر ابنك فليراجعها، وكان قد طلقها حائض (۱).
فإن قيل: الأمر إنما أثبت الوجوب على عمر أن يأمر ابنه بالمراجعة، فكيف يثبت وجوب المراجعة بقول عمر؟

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the timing of a divorce after a revocable divorce issued during menstruation. It begins with the ruling that after the woman completes two menstrual cycles (i.e., one menstruation and one purity following the divorce), the husband has the choice to either keep her or divorce her in accordance with the Sunnah. The author then discusses the obligation of revocation: some say it is recommended, but the correct view is that it is obligatory, based on the Prophet's command to Umar to instruct his son to take back his wife whom he had divorced while she was menstruating. The passage addresses an objection: the command was to Umar to command his son, so how does that prove obligation on the son? The answer is that the deputy's action is as the principal's, so it is as if the Prophet directly commanded the son. Then al-Khujandi's statement about khul' during menstruation is mentioned. The core of the dispute comes when the matn states that after the woman becomes pure, menstruates, and becomes pure again, the husband has the choice. This is the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Abu Hanifa and Zufar differ: if the husband takes her back by word after divorcing her in menstruation, he may divorce her in the purity immediately following that menstruation, without waiting for another cycle. The passage then extends this dispute to a similar scenario where the divorce was in a purity without intercourse. Abu Yusuf disagrees with Abu Hanifa. There is confusion about Muhammad's position: al-Tahawi says he agrees with Abu Hanifa, while Abu al-Layth says he agrees with Abu Yusuf. The same disagreement applies if revocation is by touch, kiss, or looking. However, if revocation is by intercourse, all agree that he cannot divorce her in that same purity.

Key Terms
الخلع (al-khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation
الرجعة (al-raj'ah): Revocation of a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) within the waiting period
المراجعة (al-muraja'ah): Same as raj'ah; used interchangeably in the text

After a divorce during menstruation, revocation is wajib, but the timing of a subsequent divorce depends on the method of revocation and differs among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.

Who Can Divorce: The General Rule and Its Exceptions
Statement of the general rule and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule of valid divorce and identify the categories of people whose divorce is invalid, explaining the reasoning for each exception.

Core Idea
Divorce is valid from an adult sane man, even if coerced or joking, but invalid from a minor, insane, sleeping, unconscious, or drugged person.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira expands on the matn's statement about who can pronounce a valid divorce. It clarifies that the default is validity for any adult sane husband, regardless of circumstances like coercion or jest, but then lists several exceptions. Understanding these exceptions is crucial because they hinge on the fundamental requirement of legal capacity and choice (ikhtiyar) for the pronouncement of talaq to have legal effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The text states that divorce takes effect from every husband who is baligh (adult) and 'aqil (sane). This includes free and slave, willing and coerced, joking and serious.
The rule is broad; only lack of capacity or choice invalidates.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith 'Every divorce is permissible except the divorce of a minor and an insane person' is cited as proof. This is a textual evidence from the Sunnah.
The hadith is reported as mawquf but considered sahih by the commentator.
3
List the exceptions explicitly mentioned
The hadith explicitly excludes minors and the insane. The commentator adds: the ma'tuh (feeble-minded) in a state of infirmity, the sleeping person, the unconscious, and one who has drunk bang (a narcotic).
These are all cases where the husband lacks sound speech or choice.
4
Analyze the underlying rationale
The minor and insane have no valid speech (qawl). The sleeping, unconscious, and drugged lack choice (ikhtiyar). The ma'tuh's divorce is valid only when lucid.
The common thread is the absence of deliberate, conscious pronouncement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ويقع طلاق كل زوج إذا كان بالغاً عاقلاً)، سواء كان حراً أو عبداً، طائعاً أو مكرهاً، هازلاً كان أو جاداً ؛ لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: كل الطلاق جائز إلا طلاق الصبي والمجنون (۱). صحيح.
قوله: (ولا يقعُ طلاق الصبي والمجنون)؛ لأنه ليس لهما قول، وكذا المعتوه لا يقعُ طلاقه أيضاً، وهو: من كان مختلط بعض كلامه مثل كلام العقلاء، وبعضه مثل كلام المجانين. وهذا إذا كان في حال العته، أما في حالة الإفاقة: فالصحيح أنه واقع. (و) كذا (النائم) لا يقع طلاقه ؛ لأنه عديم الاختيار. وكذا المغمى عليه، ومَن شرب البَنْجَ.
His statement: (And the divorce of every husband takes effect if he is an adult, sane), whether free or slave, willingly or coerced, joking or serious; due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Every divorce is permissible except the divorce of a minor and an insane person' (1). It is authentic. His statement: (And the divorce of a minor and an insane person does not take effect) because they have no valid statement. Likewise, the divorce of a feeble-minded person (ma'tuh) does not take effect either, and he is one whose speech is mixed: part like the speech of sane people and part like the speech of madmen. This is when he is in a state of feeble-mindedness; but in a state of lucidity, the correct view is it does take effect. And likewise, the sleeping person's divorce does not take effect because he lacks choice. Likewise, the unconscious person, and one who has drunk bang (a narcotic).
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentator expanding on the matn's words. It states the general rule: divorce is valid from any husband who is an adult ('aqil) and sane (baligh). This applies regardless of status (free or slave), will (voluntary or coerced), or intent (joking or serious). The proof is the hadith: 'Every divorce is permissible except the divorce of a minor and an insane person.' Then it lists the exceptions: divorce does not take effect from a minor (sabi) or an insane person (majnun) because they lack valid speech (qawl). The same applies to a 'ma'tuh' (feeble-minded) whose speech is inconsistent; but if he is in a lucid state, his divorce is effective. Also, a sleeping person's divorce is invalid because he lacks choice (ikhtiyar). Similarly, an unconscious person (mughtama 'alayh) and one who has taken bang (a narcotic substance that affects the mind) are included in the ruling of no divorce.

Key Terms
الصبي (al-sabi): A minor who has not reached puberty; his divorce is invalid because he lacks legal capacity (ahliyyah al-ada').
المجنون (al-majnun): An insane person who lacks reasoning; his divorce is invalid because he has no valid speech (qawl).
المعتوه (al-ma'tuh): A person with intermittent mental incapacity (feeble-minded); his divorce is invalid during his state of infirmity, but valid during lucidity.
البنج (al-banj): A narcotic substance (like henbane) that induces intoxication or unconsciousness; a person who consumes it loses choice and his divorce is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the divorce of a person who has drunk bang (a narcotic)?
A) It is valid because he is an adult
B) It is invalid because he lacks choice
C) It is valid only if he intended to divorce
D) It is invalid only if he drank it willingly

Key Takeaway
Divorce is valid from any adult sane husband, but invalid from those who lack valid speech or choice, such as minors, the insane, the sleeping, the unconscious, and the drugged.

Conditions for Valid Divorce: Adult, Sane, and Conscious
Ruling with proof on talaq conditions and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for a valid divorce (baligh, 'aqil, conscious) and explain why a sleeping person's divorce is invalid according to the Hanafi school, citing the hadith of the minor and insane.

Core Idea
Divorce is valid only from an adult, sane, conscious husband — sleeping, insane, or minor pronouncements are void.

This lesson examines the essential conditions a husband must meet for his divorce to take effect. The Hanafi school, based on a hadith of the Prophet (saw), requires the husband to be adult and sane. The text also addresses borderline cases: the sleeping, unconscious, and intoxicated, where an intra-Hanafi dispute arises. Additionally, we consider the case of a slave husband.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
The text states that divorce takes effect only if the husband is adult (baligh) and sane ('aqil). It also implies consciousness by excluding the sleeping person.
Consciousness is derived from the invalidity of the sleeping person's divorce.
2
Locate the hadith proof
The hadith 'All divorce is permissible except the divorce of the minor and the insane' is cited. This shows the general rule and the exceptions.
The hadith is narrated in Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq and is considered ahad (singular transmission), thus zanni al-thubut.
3
Analyze the exception
The exception proves that all other divorces are valid, implying that adulthood and sanity are necessary conditions. The minor lacks legal capacity; the insane lacks intellect.
The ma'tuh (idiot with mixed speech) is included in the insane, but his divorce in lucid intervals is valid.
4
Examine the sleeping person dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that a sleeping person's divorce is invalid due to lack of choice (ikhtiyar). Abu Yusuf differs. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's.
The dispute also extends to revocation by touch, kiss, or looking, but not by intercourse (consensus).
5
Apply to the slave scenario
A slave who marries with his master's permission has the right to divorce. The master cannot divorce the slave's wife, as per the hadith 'Divorce is in the hand of the one who owns the leg'.
The master does not own the marital bond; it belongs to the slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the divorce of every husband occurs if he is adult, sane. And the divorce of the minor, the insane, and the sleeping person does not occur. And Abu Yusuf said: He does not have that. And the statement of Muhammad is inconsistent: al-Tahawi mentioned that he is with Abu Hanifa, and Abu al-Layth mentioned that he is with Abu Yusuf. And similarly the idiot (ma'tuh) – his divorce also does not occur, and he is one whose speech is mixed: some like the speech of sane people and some like the speech of madmen. Likewise the one who is unconscious, and the one who drank bang (hashish). And if divorce occurs on the tongue of a sleeping person, it is not considered. And if he wakes up and says, 'I permitted that divorce' or 'I pronounced it,' it does not occur because he referred back to something not considered. His saying: 'And if the slave marries' – then he divorces his wife, his divorce occurs because his statement is valid if it does not affect the right of his master, and the master has no right in this marriage. His saying: 'And the divorce of his master on his wife does not occur' due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Divorce is in the hand of the one who owns the leg.' And because the right to dissolution was obtained for the slave, so its removal is up to him.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the conditions required for a valid divorce pronouncement. The author states that divorce takes effect only from a husband who is adult and sane. The divorce of a minor, insane person, or sleeping person does not take effect. An intra-Hanafi dispute is mentioned: Abu Yusuf differs on the sleeping person's divorce, and Muhammad's position is reported inconsistently. The dispute extends to revocation (رجعة) by touch, kiss, or looking at the vagina, but revocation by intercourse is agreed to be effective. The commentary emphasizes that the conditions apply whether the husband is free or slave, willing or coerced, joking or serious, based on the hadith 'All divorce is permissible except the divorce of the minor and the insane.' The insane person is further defined: includes the ma'tuh (one with mixed sanity) and also the sleeping, unconscious, and intoxicated. If a sleeping person utters divorce, it is ignored; even if he later affirms it, it does not count. Regarding a slave who marries with his master's permission, his divorce is valid, but the master cannot divorce the slave's wife, as per the hadith 'Divorce is in the hand of the one who owns the leg.' The master has no right over the marriage.

الصبي (al-sabi): Minor boy who has not reached puberty.
المجنون (al-majnoon): Insane person, lacking intellect.
النائم (al-na'im): Sleeping person, lacking consciousness and choice.
الطلاق بيد من ملك الساق (al-talaq bi yadi man malaka al-saq): The hadith: 'Divorce is in the hand of the one who owns the leg' meaning the husband who owns the wife's leg (i.e., the one who has marital authority).

Explicit Divorce (Sarih): Definition and Examples
Definition of explicit divorce with linguistic explanation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define explicit divorce (sarih), identify its examples, and explain why certain words are considered sarih.

Core Idea
Explicit divorce words effect divorce instantly without need for intent.

Divorce in Islamic law can be expressed through clear or ambiguous words. This lesson focuses on the first category: explicit (sarih) divorce. Understanding sarih is essential because these pronouncements are legally binding immediately, regardless of the husband's inner intent. We will dissect the linguistic root and examples provided by the classical text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of divorce
The passage states that divorce is of two types: sarih and kinayah. Note that this is a binary classification in Hanafi fiqh.
Sarih is the focus; kinayah will be covered separately.
2
Define sarih linguistically and legally
Sarih means 'clear' or 'apparent.' The passage says it is 'what has its intended meaning apparent, clearly.' Legally, it means the word itself unambiguously signifies divorce.
The root is derived from a tall palace (sarh) – a metaphor for clarity.
3
Analyze the examples provided
The author gives two examples: 'Anti taliq' (You are divorced) and 'Anti hurrah' (You are free). Understand why each is sarih: the first directly states divorce, the second (in context) clearly indicates separation.
Other words like 'You are released' may also be sarih in context.
4
Apply the definition to distinguish from kinayah
A word is sarih if it has no plausible meaning other than divorce. If it could mean something else (e.g., 'Go to your family'), it is kinayah and requires intent.
This distinction determines whether nīyah (intent) is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( والطلاق على ضربين: صريح، وكناية). فالصريح ما ظَهَرَ المراد به ظهوراً ،بيِّناً، مثل: أنت طالق، أنت حرة، ومنه: سُمِّي القصر : صَرْحاً؛ لارتفاعه على سائر الأبنية.
His saying: 'And divorce is of two types: explicit and implicit.' The explicit is what has its intended meaning apparent, clearly, like: 'You are divorced,' 'You are free.' And from this, a palace is called 'sarh' because it rises above other buildings.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage defines the two categories of divorce: explicit (sarih) and implicit (kinayah). Explicit divorce uses words that have a clear and unambiguous meaning of divorce, such as 'Anti taliq' (You are divorced) or 'Anti hurrah' (You are free, in the context of divorce). The term 'sarih' is derived from the word for a tall palace (sarh), indicating clarity and elevation. Implicit (kinayah) divorce uses words that can imply divorce but are not specific; they require intent to effect divorce. The passage only defines sarih; kinayah is mentioned but not elaborated here.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): Explicit: a word whose intended meaning (divorce) is clearly and unambiguously apparent.
كناية (kinayah): Implicit: a word that can mean divorce but is ambiguous; intent must be proven.
أنت طالق (anti taliq): You are divorced (said to a woman).
أنت حرة (anti hurrah): You are free; here used in the context of divorce (giving freedom from marriage).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) 'I have no need of you'
D) 'You are not my wife'

Key Takeaway
Explicit (sarih) divorce words are legally conclusive and require no proof of intent, making them distinct from implicit (kinayah) words.

Explicit Words of Divorce: Hanafi Rule and Shafi'i Dispute
Defining explicit divorce words and their legal effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on explicit divorce words, explain the reliance on customary usage (ʿurf), and distinguish the Shafiʿī position on the role of intention.

Core Idea
Explicit divorce words effect one revocable divorce—intention cannot increase the number.

This lesson unpacks a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafiʿī schools: the effect of explicit (ṣarīḥ) divorce words. While both schools recognize these words as effectual, they diverge on whether the husband's intention can alter the number of divorces. The Hanafi position, grounded in customary usage of the Arabic language, holds that only one revocable divorce occurs regardless of inner intention. Understanding this ruling is crucial for accurate fatwa and judicial decisions.

Guided Path
1
Identify Explicit Words and Their Ruling
From the passage, list the recognized explicit words (e.g., 'anti ṭāliq') and note that they effect a revocable divorce (ṭalāq rajʿī) instantly.
These words are exclusively used for divorce in Arabic custom.
2
Explain the Hanafi Proof: Customary Usage (ʿUrf)
The text cites the reason: 'because these words are used for divorce and not for other things.' This ʿurf-based proof is zanni in transmission but qatʿī in meaning—the words are unambiguous and limited to a single divorce.
ʿUrf here is the dominant linguistic practice of Arabs.
3
Understand the Dispute: Role of Intention
The Hanafis state that even if the husband intends more than one divorce, only one occurs. Imam al-Shāfiʿī disagreed, holding that the number follows the intention.
This is a dispute over whether custom (ʿurf) or intention (niyyah) governs explicit utterances.
4
Evaluate the Practical Difference (Thamarah al-Khilāf)
In the Hanafi school, a husband who says 'anti ṭāliq' intending three divorces only effects one revocable divorce. In the Shāfiʿī school, three irrevocable divorces would occur.
The consequence is significant for the marital bond and possibility of reconciliation.
5
Note the Muftā Bihī Position
The Hanafi position—one revocable divorce, no role for intention—is the relied-upon view (muftā bihī) in the school.
This is recorded in the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالصريح قوله : أنتِ طالق، و : مُطَلَّقَةٌ، و : قد طلقتك، فهذا يقع به الطلاق الرجعي، ولا يقعُ به إلا واحدةً وإن نوى أكثر من ذلك، ولا يفتقر بهذه الألفاظ إلى النية . والكناية : ما استتر المراد به.
قوله: (فالصريح: قوله : أنت طالق، و: مطلقة، و: قد طلقتك، فهذا يقع به الطلاق الرجعي)؛ لأن هذه الألفاظ تُستعمل في الطلاق، ولا تُستعمل في غيره.
* قوله : (ولا يقع به إلا واحدةً وإن نوى أكثر من ذلك). (1) وقال الشافعي : يقع ما نوى.
قوله: (ولا يفتقر إلى النية) يعني الصريح؛ لغلبة الاستعمال.
(1) مغني المحتاج ٢٩٤/٣.
So the explicit is his saying: 'You are divorced', and 'divorced', and 'I have divorced you'. So by this a revocable divorce occurs, and only one occurs even if he intended more than that, and these words do not require intention. And the implicit is: that whose meaning is hidden. He said: (The explicit: his saying: 'You are divorced', and 'divorced', and 'I have divorced you', so by this a revocable divorce occurs) because these words are used for divorce and not used for other than it. He said: (And only one occurs even if he intended more than that). (1) And al-Shafi'i said: What he intended occurs. He said: (And it does not need intention) meaning the explicit, due to prevalence of usage.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage defines the two categories of divorce wording: explicit (ṣarīḥ) and implicit (kināyah). Explicit words are those that are exclusively used for divorce, such as 'You are divorced' (anti ṭāliq), 'divorced' (muṭallaqah), or 'I have divorced you' (qad ṭallaqtuk). When a husband uses these words, a revocable divorce (ṭalāq rajʿī) takes effect immediately, and only one divorce occurs regardless of his intention, even if he intended two or three. This is because these words are not customarily used for more than one. The text also clarifies that no explicit intention is needed for these words to be effective, due to their dominant usage. Imam al-Shafiʿī disagreed, holding that the number of divorces depends on the husband's intention. The Hanafi position is that the words themselves are limited to one, as they are not employed to indicate multiplicity in common speech. A footnote references Mughnī al-Muḥtāj for further detail.

Key Terms
الصريح (al-ṣarīḥ): Words that are clearly and exclusively used for divorce, such as 'ṭāliq' and its derivatives.
الكناية (al-kināyah): Words that are ambiguous and require intention (niyyah) to effect divorce.
الطلاق الرجعي (al-ṭalāq al-rajʿī): A revocable divorce during the waiting period (ʿiddah), where the husband can take back his wife without a new contract.
نية (niyyah): Intent; for explicit divorce words, niyyah is not required for the divorce to take effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) One revocable
C) One irrevocable
D) None because intent not expressed

Key Takeaway
Explicit divorce words (ṣarīḥ) effect a single revocable divorce in the Hanafi school, and the husband’s intention cannot increase the number.

Default Rulings on Ambiguous Divorce Utterances (أنت الطلاق etc.)
Ruling statement with intra-Hanafi disputation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default ruling for each of the three ambiguous divorce utterances and explain why intention for two is invalid for a free woman but valid for a slave woman, citing the Hanafi response to Zufar's counterargument.

Core Idea
For ambiguous divorce utterances, default is one revocable; intention for three is valid due to genus noun, but for two it is not.

The passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyirah addresses the most common ambiguous divorce phrases. It clarifies that without intention, one revocable divorce occurs, and that the husband's intention for a specific number is effective only for three, not two. This section also resolves the objection raised by Zufar using the linguistic nature of the sources noun.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main phrases
List the utterances 'أنتِ الطلاق', 'أنت طالق الطلاق', and 'أنت طالق طلاقاً'. Note that they all default to one revocable divorce when no intention is present.
These are key ambiguous phrases in Hanafi divorce law.
2
Understand the default ruling
Explain that without intention, only one revocable divorce occurs. This is the baseline.
The verbal noun (مصدر) 'طلاق' functions as a genus noun, not indicating a number.
3
Examine intention for two
State that if the husband intends two divorces, it does not take effect for a free woman. The reason is that 'two' is a specific number not indicated by the word.
Contrast with Zufar's view.
4
Analyze intention for three
Intention for three is valid because the verbal noun implies multiplicity (كثرة) as a genus noun.
This is the majority Hanafi position.
5
Address Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that since intention for three is valid, intention for two (a part of three) should also be valid. The Hanafi response distinguishes: validity for three is due to genus, not part-whole. For a slave woman, intention for two is valid because genus is considered; for a free woman, two is a specific number.
This shows the importance of linguistic analysis in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق
وقوله : أنتِ الطلاق، أو : أنتِ طالق الطلاق، أو : أنت طالق طلاقاً : فإن لم تكن له نية : فهي واحدة رجعية، وإن نوى اثنتين : لم يقع إلا واحدةً رجعية، وإن نوى به ثلاثاً : كان ثلاثاً .
ولو قال: أنت مطلقة بتسكين الطاء والتخفيف: لا يكون طلاقاً إلا بالنية.
ولو طلقها طلقةً رجعية، ثم قال : جعلتُها بائناً، أو ثلاثاً: صار كذلك عند أبي حنيفة.
وقال أبو يوسف: تصير بائناً، ولا تصير ثلاثاً.
وقال محمد وزفر : لا تصير بائناً، ولا ثلاثاً.
ولو قال لها : كوني طالقاً، أو أطلقي: قال محمد : أراه واقعاً. وكذا إذا قال لأمته: كوني حرة أو : اعتقي.
قوله: (وقوله : أنت الطالق، أو أنت طالق الطلاق، أو: أنت طالق طلاقاً، فإن لم تكن له نيةٌ فهي واحدة رجعية، وإن نوى اثنتين: فهي واحدة رجعية أيضاً، وإن نوى ثلاثاً : فهي ثلاث).
وكذا إذا قال: أنتِ طلاق يقع به الطلاق أيضاً، ولا يحتاج فيه إلى نية، ويكون رجعياً.
وتصح نية الثلاث فيه؛ لأن المصدر يحتمل العموم والكثرة؛ لأنه اسم جنس.
ولا تصح نية الثنتين فيه، خلافاً لزفر.
هو يقول : إن الثنتين بعض الثلاث، فلما صحت نية الثلاث: صحت نية بعضها.
ونحن نقول: نية الثلاث إنما صحت لكونها جنساً، حتى لو كانت المرأة أمةً : تصح نية الثنتين؛ باعتبار الجنسية، أما الثنتان في حق الحرة: عدد، واللفظ لا يحتمل العدد.
ولو قال: أنتِ طالق ،الطلاق، وقال أردت بقولي: طالق واحدةً، وبقولي: الطلاق أخرى صدق؛ لأن كل واحدة منهما صالحة للإيقاع، فكأنه قال : أنتِ طالق وطالق، فيقع رجعيتان إذا كانت مدخولاً بها.
* قوله: (وإن نوى اثنتين: لم يقع إلا واحدةً).
هذا إذا كانت حرة، أما إذا كانت أمةً: يقع ثنتان، وتحرم، أو يكون قد تقدم على الحرة واحدة: فيقع اثنتان إذا نواهما، يعني مع الأُولى.
ولو قال: أنت طالق طلاقاً ولا نية له : وقعت واحدة؛ لأن المصدر إنما يفيد التأكيد، لا غير، كقولك قمتُ قياماً، وأكلتُ أكلاً، والتأكيد لا يفيد إلا ما أفاده المؤكد.
وإن نوى ثلاثاً: كان ثلاثاً، في رواية «الأصل»؛ لأن المصدر يفيد معنى الكثرة.
وعن أبي حنيفة : لا يقع إلا واحدة.
ولو قال : يا مطلقة : بالتشديد : وقع عليها الطلاق؛ لأنه وَصَفَها بذلك.
Book of Divorce. And his statement: 'You are divorce' or 'You are divorced the divorce' or 'You are divorced a divorce': if he has no intention, then it is one revocable [divorce]; and if he intends two, then only one revocable occurs; and if he intends three, then it is three. And if he says: 'You are divorced' with sukun on the ta and lightening (takhfif), it is not divorce except with intention. And if he divorced her with one revocable divorce, then said: 'I made it irrevocable' or 'three', it becomes so according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: It becomes irrevocable, but not three. Muhammad and Zufar said: It does not become irrevocable nor three. And if he said to her: 'Be divorced' or 'be released', Muhammad said: I see it occurring. Similarly if he said to his slave-girl: 'Be free' or 'be emancipated'. He said: (And his statement: 'You are the divorced', or 'you are divorced the divorce', or 'you are divorced a divorce', if he has no intention then it is one revocable, and if he intends two then it is one revocable also, and if he intends three then it is three). Similarly if he says: 'You are divorce', divorce occurs with it also, and does not need intention, and it is revocable. Intention for three is valid in it because the verbal noun admits generality and multiplicity as it is a genus noun. Intention for two is not valid in it, contrary to Zufar. He says: Two is part of three; so when intention for three is valid, intention for part of it is valid. And we say: Intention for three is valid because of it being a genus, such that if the woman were a slave-girl, intention for two is valid by consideration of genus; but two with respect to a free woman is a number, and the word does not accept number. And if he said: 'You are divorced, the divorce', and said: 'I meant by my saying "divorced" one, and by my saying "the divorce" another', he is believed because each one is suitable for effectuation; it is as if he said: 'You are divorced and divorced', so two revocable divorces occur if she is one with whom he has consummated marriage. * His statement: (And if he intends two, only one occurs). This is if she is free; but if she is a slave-girl, two occur and she becomes forbidden, or if one divorce had preceded regarding a free woman, then two occur if he intends them, meaning along with the first. And if he said: 'You are divorced a divorce' with no intention, one occurs because the verbal noun only gives emphasis, nothing else, like your saying: 'I stood a standing' and 'I ate an eating'; emphasis only conveys what the emphasized conveys. And if he intends three, it is three in the narration of al-Asl because the verbal noun conveys the meaning of multiplicity. And from Abu Hanifa: Only one occurs. And if he said: 'O divorced', with tashdid, divorce occurs upon her because he described her with that.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri discusses various utterances of divorce and the role of intention. The passage type is a combination of rulings and intra-Hanafi disputes. The author first presents the matn: if a husband says 'You are the divorce', 'You are divorced the divorce', or 'You are divorced a divorce', without intention, one revocable divorce occurs; if he intends two, only one revocable occurs; if he intends three, three occur. This is because the word 'الطلاق' (the divorce) or 'طلاقاً' (a divorce) is a verbal noun that can indicate multiplicity when intended, but without intention it defaults to a single revocable divorce. Then the author adds that if he says 'You are divorced' with light pronunciation (sukun and takhfif), it is not divorce unless he intends it, because the utterance is ambiguous. Next, the passage addresses the case where a man gives a revocable divorce and then says 'I made it irrevocable' or 'three'. According to Abu Hanifa, his statement makes it so. Abu Yusuf agrees that it becomes irrevocable but not triple. Muhammad and Zufar hold that it does not change at all. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. Then the author discusses the phrase 'Be divorced' or 'Be released'; Muhammad considers it effective, similar to saying to a slave-girl 'Be free'. The commentary then revisits the main matn, explaining that saying 'You are divorce' (without 'the' or 'a') also effects a revocable divorce without need for intention. Intention for three is valid in such expressions because the source noun (مصدر) implies generality and multiplicity, being a noun for the genus. Intention for two is not valid, contrary to Zufar who argues that two is part of three. The Hanafi response: intention for three is valid due to genus; for a slave-woman, intention for two is also valid because the genus is respected; but for a free woman, two is a specific number not indicated by the word. Then the author presents a scenario: if he says 'You are divorced, the divorce' and intends each part for a different divorce, he is believed and two revocable divorces occur if the marriage was consummated. Next, the commentary clarifies that the ruling 'if he intends two only one occurs' applies to a free woman; for a slave-woman, two occur and she becomes forbidden. If a free woman already had one divorce, intending two would make a total of three, forbidding her. Then the author discusses 'You are divorced a divorce' with no intention: only one occurs because the verbal noun is for emphasis, like 'I stood a standing'. If he intends three, according to the narration of al-Asl (the foundational work), three occur because the verbal noun conveys multiplicity. However, there is another narration from Abu Hanifa that only one occurs. Finally, if he says 'O divorced' with tashdid, divorce occurs because he described her as such.

Key Terms
طلقة رجعية (talaqa raj'iyya): A revocable divorce where the husband can take his wife back during the iddah without a new marriage.
بائن (bā'in): An irrevocable divorce where the husband cannot take his wife back without a new marriage and consent.
نية (niyya): The husband's intention when pronouncing divorce, which can affect the number and type of divorce.
مصدر (masdar): Verbal noun; in this context, 'طلاقاً' is a masdar indicating the act of divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a husband says to his free wife 'أنتِ الطلاق' intending two divorces, what is the outcome?
A) One revocable divorce.
B) Two revocable divorces.
C) Three divorces.
D) No divorce.

Key Takeaway
In ambiguous divorce phrases, intention is effective only for three, not two, due to the verbal noun's genus nature.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Changing a Revocable Divorce Through Subsequent Words and Light Pronunciation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on subsequent statements and ruling on light pronunciation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether saying 'جعلتها بائناً أو ثلاثاً' after a revocable divorce changes its nature, and identify the ruling for the light pronunciation 'أنت مطلقة'.

Core Idea
After a revocable divorce, a husband's statement can make it irrevocable or triple according to Abu Hanifa, but only irrevocable according to Abu Yusuf, and not at all according to Muhammad and Zufar.

This section of the passage deals with two distinct issues: the effect of a husband's subsequent statement on a revocable divorce, and the rule for the lightly pronounced word 'أنت مطلقة'. These rulings highlight how the school's founders disagreed on the power of later intent to alter a divorce's character.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A husband gives a single revocable divorce. He then says 'I made it irrevocable' or 'I made it three'. The question: does this change the divorce's status?
This is a kalam lāḥiq (subsequent statement).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that the divorce becomes what the husband declared: irrevocable or triple.
This gives the husband ability to upgrade the divorce after the fact.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf agrees that it becomes irrevocable (bā'in), but not triple (three).
He distinguishes between irrevocable and triple.
4
State Muhammad and Zufar's position
Muhammad and Zufar hold that it does not change at all; it remains a revocable divorce.
They argue that a subsequent statement cannot alter an already completed divorce.
5
Analyze the light pronunciation issue
If the husband says 'أنت مطلقة' with sukun on the ṭā and takhfīf, it is not a divorce unless he intends it. This is because the light pronunciation is ambiguous; intention clarifies it.
Contrast with the heavier forms discussed earlier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق
وقوله : أنتِ الطلاق، أو : أنتِ طالق الطلاق، أو : أنت طالق طلاقاً : فإن لم تكن له نية : فهي واحدة رجعية، وإن نوى اثنتين : لم يقع إلا واحدةً رجعية، وإن نوى به ثلاثاً : كان ثلاثاً .
ولو قال: أنت مطلقة بتسكين الطاء والتخفيف: لا يكون طلاقاً إلا بالنية.
ولو طلقها طلقةً رجعية، ثم قال : جعلتُها بائناً، أو ثلاثاً: صار كذلك عند أبي حنيفة.
وقال أبو يوسف: تصير بائناً، ولا تصير ثلاثاً.
وقال محمد وزفر : لا تصير بائناً، ولا ثلاثاً.
ولو قال لها : كوني طالقاً، أو أطلقي: قال محمد : أراه واقعاً. وكذا إذا قال لأمته: كوني حرة أو : اعتقي.
قوله: (وقوله : أنت الطالق، أو أنت طالق الطلاق، أو: أنت طالق طلاقاً، فإن لم تكن له نيةٌ فهي واحدة رجعية، وإن نوى اثنتين: فهي واحدة رجعية أيضاً، وإن نوى ثلاثاً : فهي ثلاث).
وكذا إذا قال: أنتِ طلاق يقع به الطلاق أيضاً، ولا يحتاج فيه إلى نية، ويكون رجعياً.
وتصح نية الثلاث فيه؛ لأن المصدر يحتمل العموم والكثرة؛ لأنه اسم جنس.
ولا تصح نية الثنتين فيه، خلافاً لزفر.
هو يقول : إن الثنتين بعض الثلاث، فلما صحت نية الثلاث: صحت نية بعضها.
ونحن نقول: نية الثلاث إنما صحت لكونها جنساً، حتى لو كانت المرأة أمةً : تصح نية الثنتين؛ باعتبار الجنسية، أما الثنتان في حق الحرة: عدد، واللفظ لا يحتمل العدد.
ولو قال: أنتِ طالق ،الطلاق، وقال أردت بقولي: طالق واحدةً، وبقولي: الطلاق أخرى صدق؛ لأن كل واحدة منهما صالحة للإيقاع، فكأنه قال : أنتِ طالق وطالق، فيقع رجعيتان إذا كانت مدخولاً بها.
* قوله: (وإن نوى اثنتين: لم يقع إلا واحدةً).
هذا إذا كانت حرة، أما إذا كانت أمةً: يقع ثنتان، وتحرم، أو يكون قد تقدم على الحرة واحدة: فيقع اثنتان إذا نواهما، يعني مع الأُولى.
ولو قال: أنت طالق طلاقاً ولا نية له : وقعت واحدة؛ لأن المصدر إنما يفيد التأكيد، لا غير، كقولك قمتُ قياماً، وأكلتُ أكلاً، والتأكيد لا يفيد إلا ما أفاده المؤكد.
وإن نوى ثلاثاً: كان ثلاثاً، في رواية «الأصل»؛ لأن المصدر يفيد معنى الكثرة.
وعن أبي حنيفة : لا يقع إلا واحدة.
ولو قال : يا مطلقة : بالتشديد : وقع عليها الطلاق؛ لأنه وَصَفَها بذلك.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
6 min
Emphasis in 'أنت طالق طلاقاً' and the Case of 'يا مطلقة'
Ruling statement with intra-Hanafi narration variance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the emphatic use of the verbal noun in 'أنت طالق طلاقاً' and its potential for multiplicity when intended, and identify when calling a woman 'يا مطلقة' effects divorce.

Core Idea
Without intention, 'أنت طالق طلاقاً' is mere emphasis and one divorce occurs; with intention for three, it is three according to al-Asl, but one according to a narration from Abu Hanifa.

In this closing section of the passage, the author elaborates on the phrase 'أنت طالق طلاقاً' already mentioned earlier. Here the focus is on the linguistic concept of emphasis (تأكيد) and how it does not add a new divorce by itself. Additionally, the rule for calling a woman 'يا مطلقة' with tashdid is presented.

Guided Path
1
Understand the emphatic construction
When a verbal noun follows its verb, like 'طلقت طلاقاً' or 'أنت طالق طلاقاً', it is considered emphatic (تأكيد). It confirms the action but does not add a new divorce.
Similar to 'قمت قياماً' – emphasis only.
2
Default ruling with no intention
If the husband says this without intention, only one divorce occurs, because the verbal noun is merely emphatic.
Contrast with the earlier case where intention for three was valid.
3
Intention for three: two narrations
In the narration of al-Asl, if he intends three, three occur because the verbal noun can indicate multiplicity. Another narration from Abu Hanifa states only one occurs.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf on the effect of intention in this specific construction.
4
The case of 'يا مطلقة' with tashdid
Calling a woman 'يا مطلقة' (O divorced) with tashdid on the ṭā' effects divorce because it directly describes her as divorced, implying the divorce has occurred.
The tashdid makes it a direct address and description, not just a vocative.

Source Text

And we say: Intention for three is valid because of it being a genus, such that if the woman were a slave-girl, intention for two is valid by consideration of genus; but two with respect to a free woman is a number, and the word does not accept number. And if he said: 'You are divorced, the divorce', and said: 'I meant by my saying "divorced" one, and by my saying "the divorce" another', he is believed because each one is suitable for effectuation; it is as if he said: 'You are divorced and divorced', so two revocable divorces occur if she is one with whom he has consummated marriage. * His statement: (And if he intends two, only one occurs). This is if she is free; but if she is a slave-girl, two occur and she becomes forbidden, or if one divorce had preceded regarding a free woman, then two occur if he intends them, meaning along with the first. And if he said: 'You are divorced a divorce' with no intention, one occurs because the verbal noun only gives emphasis, nothing else, like your saying: 'I stood a standing' and 'I ate an eating'; emphasis only conveys what the emphasized conveys. And if he intends three, it is three in the narration of al-Asl because the verbal noun conveys the meaning of multiplicity. And from Abu Hanifa: Only one occurs. And if he said: 'O divorced', with tashdid, divorce occurs upon her because he described her with that.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri discusses various utterances of divorce and the role of intention. The passage type is a combination of rulings and intra-Hanafi disputes. The author first presents the matn: if a husband says 'You are the divorce', 'You are divorced the divorce', or 'You are divorced a divorce', without intention, one revocable divorce occurs; if he intends two, only one revocable occurs; if he intends three, three occur. This is because the word 'الطلاق' (the divorce) or 'طلاقاً' (a divorce) is a verbal noun that can indicate multiplicity when intended, but without intention it defaults to a single revocable divorce. Then the author adds that if he says 'You are divorced' with light pronunciation (sukun and takhfif), it is not divorce unless he intends it, because the utterance is ambiguous. Next, the passage addresses the case where a man gives a revocable divorce and then says 'I made it irrevocable' or 'three'. According to Abu Hanifa, his statement makes it so. Abu Yusuf agrees that it becomes irrevocable but not triple. Muhammad and Zufar hold that it does not change at all. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. Then the author discusses the phrase 'Be divorced' or 'Be released'; Muhammad considers it effective, similar to saying to a slave-girl 'Be free'. The commentary then revisits the main matn, explaining that saying 'You are divorce' (without 'the' or 'a') also effects a revocable divorce without need for intention. Intention for three is valid in such expressions because the source noun (مصدر) implies generality and multiplicity, being a noun for the genus. Intention for two is not valid, contrary to Zufar who argues that two is part of three. The Hanafi response: intention for three is valid due to genus; for a slave-woman, intention for two is also valid because the genus is respected; but for a free woman, two is a specific number not indicated by the word. Then the author presents a scenario: if he says 'You are divorced, the divorce' and intends each part for a different divorce, he is believed and two revocable divorces occur if the marriage was consummated. Next, the commentary clarifies that the ruling 'if he intends two only one occurs' applies to a free woman; for a slave-woman, two occur and she becomes forbidden. If a free woman already had one divorce, intending two would make a total of three, forbidding her. Then the author discusses 'You are divorced a divorce' with no intention: only one occurs because the verbal noun is for emphasis, like 'I stood a standing'. If he intends three, according to the narration of al-Asl (the foundational work), three occur because the verbal noun conveys multiplicity. However, there is another narration from Abu Hanifa that only one occurs. Finally, if he says 'O divorced' with tashdid, divorce occurs because he described her as such.

Key Terms
تأكيد (ta'kīd): Emphatic construction; when a verbal noun is used after its verb (e.g., 'طلقت طلاقاً'), it is considered emphatic and adds no new meaning.
مصدر (masdar): Verbal noun; in this context, 'طلاقاً' is a masdar indicating the act of divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the narration of al-Asl, if a husband says 'أنت طالق طلاقاً' while intending three divorces, what is the effect?
A) One revocable divorce.
B) Three divorces.
C) One irrevocable divorce.
D) No divorce.

Key Takeaway
Without intention, 'أنت طالق طلاقاً' is emphatic and yields one divorce; with intention for three, the relied-upon narration says three occur.
