Intention in Divorce: Intending Two or Three with 'al-Talaq'
Disputing validity of intending two divorces when saying 'al-talaq'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intending two divorces when saying 'al-talaq' and distinguish between the cases of free and slave women.

Core Idea
A word signifying a genus can bear the maximum, not a specific number.

This passage addresses a subtle issue in divorce jurisprudence: when a husband uses the word 'al-talaq' (the divorce) and intends a specific number of divorces, what falls? The Hanafi school makes a crucial distinction between a genus (jins) and a number ('adad), leading to different outcomes for free and slave women. Understanding this distinction is key to correctly applying the rulings on intention in divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The core dispute is whether intending two divorces with 'al-talaq' is valid. The opponent argues that since 'al-talaq' can bear three (as three is part of the genus), it should also bear two.
The opponent is unnamed but likely the Shafi'is.
2
State the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response relies on the distinction between genus and number. Three is a genus because it is the maximum for a free woman, while two is a specific number. The word 'al-talaq' cannot signify a specific number.
For a slave woman, two is the maximum, so it becomes a genus in that context.
3
Apply the rule to the phrase 'anti taliq al-talaq'
If a free husband says 'you are divorced, the divorce' and intends two, only one falls. If he intends three, three fall according to the narration of al-Asl, but Abu Hanifa held that only one falls.
There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on intending three.
4
Examine the indefinite 'talaqan'
The phrase 'anti taliq talaqan' (you are divorced, a divorce) without intention yields only one divorce because the verbal noun is merely emphatic.
If intention is present, it can affect the number.
5
Note the case of 'ya mutallaqa'
Saying 'O divorced woman' with tashdid effects divorce because it describes her as already divorced, which is considered a clear statement.
This is a separate, uncontested ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول : إن الثنتين بعض الثلاث، فلما صحت نية الثلاث: صحت نية بعضها.
ونحن نقول: نية الثلاث إنما صحت لكونها جنساً، حتى لو كانت المرأة أمةً : تصح نية الثنتين؛ باعتبار الجنسية، أما الثنتان في حق الحرة: عدد، واللفظ لا يحتمل العدد.
ولو قال: أنتِ طالق ،الطلاق، وقال أردت بقولي: طالق واحدةً، وبقولي: الطلاق أخرى صدق؛ لأن كل واحدة منهما صالحة للإيقاع، فكأنه قال : أنتِ طالق وطالق، فيقع رجعيتان إذا كانت مدخولاً بها.
* قوله: (وإن نوى اثنتين: لم يقع إلا واحدةً).
هذا إذا كانت حرة، أما إذا كانت أمةً: يقع ثنتان، وتحرم، أو يكون قد تقدم على الحرة واحدة: فيقع اثنتان إذا نواهما، يعني مع الأُولى. ولو قال: أنت طالق طلاقاً ولا نية له : وقعت واحدة؛ لأن المصدر إنما يفيد التأكيد، لا غير، كقولك قمتُ قياماً، وأكلتُ أكلاً، والتأكيد لا يفيد إلا ما أفاده المؤكد.
وإن نوى ثلاثاً: كان ثلاثاً، في رواية «الأصل»؛ لأن المصدر يفيد معنى الكثرة.
وعن أبي حنيفة : لا يقع إلا واحدة.
ولو قال : يا مطلقة : بالتشديد : وقع عليها الطلاق؛ لأنه وَصَفَها بذلك.
He says: Indeed, two are part of three. So when the intention of three is valid, the intention of some of it is valid. And we say: The intention of three is valid only because it is a genus, such that if the woman is a slave, the intention of two is valid due to genus-status. As for two with respect to a free woman, it is a number, and the wording does not bear a number. And if he said: 'You are divorced, the divorce', and said 'I meant by my saying 'divorced' one, and by my saying 'the divorce' another', he is truthful, because each of them is suitable for effecting divorce, so it is as if he said 'You are divorced and divorced', and two revocable divorces occur if she is one with whom he has consummated marriage. * His statement: (And if he intends two, only one falls). This is if she is free, but if she is a slave, two fall and she becomes forbidden, or if one has preceded the free woman, then two fall when he intends them, meaning together with the first. And if he said 'You are divorced, a divorce', without intention, one falls, because the verbal noun only indicates emphasis, nothing else, like your saying 'I stood, a standing' and 'I ate, an eating', and emphasis only conveys what was conveyed by the emphasized word. And if he intends three, it is three according to the narration of al-Asl, because the verbal noun indicates multiplicity. And from Abu Hanifa: only one falls. And if he said 'O divorced woman' with shaddah, divorce occurs upon her because he described her as such.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage begins with a dispute. An unnamed opponent argues that since the word 'al-talaq' can bear the intention of three divorces, it should also bear the intention of two because two are part of three. The Hanafi commentator responds by distinguishing between a genus (jins) and a number ('adad). Three is a genus because it is the maximum for a free woman, so the word 'al-talaq' can signify it. Two, however, is a specific number and cannot be signified by 'al-talaq'. The exception is a slave woman, for whom two is the maximum, making two a genus in that context. Hence, when a free husband says 'anti taliq al-talaq' and intends two, only one falls. The passage then gives additional rulings: (1) If he says 'you are divorced, the divorce' and explains his intention, he is believed. (2) The matn statement 'if he intends two, only one falls' is explained: it applies to a free woman without a prior divorce. If she is a slave or has a prior divorce, two fall. (3) The phrase 'anti taliq talaqan' (indefinite) without intention yields only one divorce because the verbal noun is merely emphatic. However, if he intends three, there is a difference: in the riwayah of al-Asl, three falls, but Abu Hanifa's own view is that only one falls. Finally, saying 'ya mutallaqa' with tashdid effects divorce because it describes her as already divorced.

Key Terms
جنس (jins): Genus; 'al-talaq' can signify the genus of divorce, which includes the maximum number (three for a free woman).
عدد ('adad): Number; two is a specific number, not a genus, so 'al-talaq' does not bear it.
نية (niyyah): Intention; the husband's unspoken intent that accompanies his words.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a free husband says 'anti taliq al-talaq' and intends two divorces, how many fall according to the Hanafi position in this passage?
A) Two
B) One
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between genus and number; 'al-talaq' can bear a genus (maximum) but not a specific number, so intending two does not effect two for a free woman.

Kinayat in Divorce: Definition and General Rule
Defining kinayat and stating the general condition for divorce through them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define kinayah, state the general condition for divorce to occur by kinayat, and distinguish between sarih and kinayah expressions.

Core Idea
Kinayat require intention or context—they are not self-interpreting like sarih words.

In Hanafi law, divorce expressions are either clear (sarih) or ambiguous (kinayah). Sarih words like 'anti taliq' effect divorce immediately without need for intention. Kinayah, however, can signify divorce or other meanings. Therefore, the husband's intention or the surrounding context determines whether divorce occurs. This lesson explains the definition and the general rule governing all kinayat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object of definition
The passage defines 'kinayah' as a linguistic category. Recognize that kinayah words are those that have multiple possible meanings, including divorce and other interpretations.
2
Understand the legal consequence of ambiguity
Because of this ambiguity, divorce does not occur automatically when a kinayah is uttered. The husband must either intend divorce (niyyah) or there must be a contextual indicator (dalalah hal) that he meant divorce.
Contextual indication could be an ongoing argument about divorce.
3
Contrast with sarih expressions
Unlike kinayah, sarih words like 'anti taliq' are unambiguous; they effect divorce even without intention. This contrast highlights the need for additional proof for kinayat.
4
Apply the rule to a hypothetical
If a husband says 'Take care of yourself' during a fight, this is a kinayah. Divorce only occurs if he intended it or the fight clearly indicates he meant divorce. Otherwise, no divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضرب الثاني : الكنايات، ولا يقعُ بها الطلاق إلا بنية، أو بدلالة حال وهي على ضربين : منها ثلاثة ألفاظ يقع بها الطلاق الرجعي، ولا يقعُ بها إلا طلقة واحدة وهي : قوله : اعتدي، و: استبرئي رَحِمَك، و : أنت واحدة.
فإن نوى ثلاثاً: كان ثلاثاً.
ولو قال: أنتِ طال: لا يقع إلا بالنية إلا في حال مذاكرة الطلاق.
ولو قال يا :طال بكسر اللام وقع الطلاق وإن لم ينوه.
ولو قال: أنت طالق طالق، أو قال: أنت طالق أنت طالق، وقال: عَنَيتُ الأول: صدق ديانةً.
وكذا إذا قال: قد طلقتك قد طلقتك، أو أنت طالق قد طلقتك، أو قال : أنت طالق، فقال له رجل: ما قلت؟ قال : قد طلقتها، أو قال: قلتُ: هي طالق فهي واحدة في القضاء.
ولو قال للمدخول بها : أنت طالق أنت، أو أنت طالق وأنت :
قال أبو يوسف: يقع واحدةً، وقال محمد: ثنتان.
* قوله: (والضرب الثاني: الكنايات ولا يقع بها الطلاق إلا بنيَّة، أو بدلالة حال؛ لأنها تحتمل الطلاق ،وغيره فلا بد من النية أو الدلالة. قوله: (وهي على ضربين منها ثلاثة ألفاظ يقع بها الطلاق الرجعي، ولا يقع بها إلا واحدةً وهي قوله: اعتدي، واستبرئي رحمك وأنت واحدة).
And the second type: kinayat, and divorce does not occur by them except with intention or contextual indication. They are of two types: among them are three expressions by which revocable divorce occurs, and only one divorce occurs by them: his saying 'i'taddi' (observe waiting period), 'istabri'i rahimak' (ascertain the purity of your womb), and 'anti wahida' (you are one). If he intends three, it is three. And if he said 'anti tali' (with a short alif), it does not occur except with intention, except in a context of discussing divorce. And if he said 'ya tali' (with kasra on the lam), divorce occurs even if he did not intend it. And if he said 'you are divorced, you are divorced' or 'you are divorced, you are divorced' and said 'I meant the first', he is truthful in private. Similarly, if he said 'I divorced you, I divorced you' or 'you are divorced, I divorced you' or he said 'you are divorced', and a man asked 'what did you say?' and he replied 'I divorced her' or said 'I said she is divorced', it is one in court. And if he said to a woman with whom consummation has occurred 'you are divorced, you' or 'you are divorced, and you', Abu Yusuf said: one occurs, and Muhammad said: two. * His statement: (And the second type: kinayat, and divorce does not occur by them except with intention or contextual indication, because they are ambiguous between divorce and other meanings, so intention or indication is necessary. His statement: (They are of two types: among them are three expressions by which revocable divorce occurs, and only one divorce occurs by them: his saying 'i'taddi', 'istabri'i rahimak', and 'anti wahida'.))
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage defines the second category of divorce expressions: kinayat (ambiguous words). Unlike sarih (explicit) words, kinayat require either the husband's intention (niyyah) or a contextual indication (dalalat hal) to effect divorce, because they can also mean other things. Among kinayat, three specific expressions—'i'taddi' (begin your 'iddah), 'istabri'i rahimak' (ascertain the purity of your womb), and 'anti wahida' (you are one)—always effect a single revocable divorce. However, if the husband intended three, his intention is accepted and three fall. The passage then addresses other ambiguous forms: (1) 'anti tali' (with short alif) only effects divorce if he intended it, unless the context is discussing divorce. (2) 'ya tali' (with kasra on lam) effects divorce even without intention because this pronunciation is strongly associated with divorce. (3) Repetition of explicit words like 'anti taliq taliq' or 'anti taliq anti taliq': if the husband says he meant the first, he is believed in private (dinan) but in court (qada') only one falls if he does not specify. (4) For the phrase 'anti taliq anti' or 'anti taliq wa-anti' to a consummated wife, Abu Yusuf rules one divorce, Muhammad rules two. The commentary concludes by repeating the matn's definition of kinayat and listing the three special words.

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): An ambiguous word that can mean divorce or something else; requires niyyah or contextual indication to effect divorce.
دلالة حال (dalalah hal): Contextual indication; e.g., a couple arguing about divorce provides context that a kinayah word is intended for divorce.
اعتدى (i'taddi): Begin your waiting period ('iddah); a kinayah that effects a revocable divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is required for a kinayah expression to effect divorce?
A) The husband must explicitly say 'I intend divorce'
B) The wife must accept the divorce
C) Either the husband's intention or contextual indication
D) The utterance must be witnessed by two men

Key Takeaway
Kinayat require either intention (niyyah) or contextual indication (dalalah hal) to effect divorce, protecting against unintended divorce due to ambiguous language.

The Three Special Kinayat and Related Rulings
Defining kinayat and listing three words that effect revocable divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three kinayat that automatically effect revocable divorce, explain how intention affects the count, and analyze the intra-Hanafi disagreement on 'anti taliq anti'.

Core Idea
Three kinayat always give one revocable divorce; other kinayat need intention or context.

Among the many kinayat words, the Hanafi school identifies three that are exceptional: 'i'taddi', 'istabri'i rahimak', and 'anti wahida'. These words, when uttered by the husband, automatically result in a single revocable divorce, even without additional intention. However, the husband's intention can increase the number of divorces. This lesson delves into these three words, the conditions for their effect, and also covers related expressions like 'anti tali' and the dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on repetition.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the three special kinayat
The three expressions are: (1) 'i'taddi' – begin your 'iddah, (2) 'istabri'i rahimak' – ascertain the purity of your womb, and (3) 'anti wahida' – you are one. Remember that these are considered strong indicators of divorce and thus effect a revocable divorce without requiring additional proof of intention.
The passage is clear that these three are among the kinayat.
2
Understand the default effect: one revocable divorce
When uttered, these words result in exactly one revocable divorce. 'Revocable' means the husband can take the wife back during the 'iddah without a new marriage contract.
3
Recognize the role of intention for the count
If the husband intended three divorces when uttering one of these words, three divorces occur. The intention is accepted.
This shows that intention can modify the number but not the fact that a divorce occurred.
4
Examine the other ambiguous expressions
The passage also discusses 'anti tali' (with short alif) and 'ya tali' (with kasra). 'Anti tali' requires intention or context, while 'ya tali' automatically effects divorce because the pronunciation strongly suggests a call to divorce.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on repetition
If a man says to his consummated wife 'anti taliq anti' or 'anti taliq wa-anti', Abu Yusuf rules one divorce, while Muhammad rules two divorces. This disagreement stems from whether the second phrase is an independent utterance or a confirmation.
The mufta bihi is not specified in this passage; both opinions exist.

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): An ambiguous word that can mean divorce or something else; requires niyyah or contextual indication to effect divorce (except three special words).
دلالة حال (dalalah hal): Contextual indication; e.g., a couple arguing about divorce provides context that a kinayah word is intended for divorce.
اعتدى (i'taddi): Begin your waiting period ('iddah); a kinayah that effects a revocable divorce automatically.
استبرئي رحمك (istabri'i rahimak): Ascertain the purity of your womb; a kinayah that effects a revocable divorce automatically.
أنت واحدة (anti wahida): You are one; a kinayah that effects a revocable divorce automatically.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'I'taddi'
B) 'Istabri'i rahimak'
C) 'Anti wahida'
D) 'Anti tali'

Key Takeaway
Three kinayat—i'taddi, istabri'i rahimak, and anti wahida—automatically effect one revocable divorce, but intention can increase the number.

Classification of Allusory Words (Kinayat) in Divorce: Hanafi Ruling
Explains the two types of kinayat and their effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three special kinayat that yield revocable divorce and all other kinayat that yield irrevocable divorce, and apply the conditions of intention and contextual indication.

Core Idea
Three allusory words yield revocable divorce; all others yield irrevocable — intention or context determines the effect.

Divorce can be expressed through explicit words (sarih) or allusory words (kinayat). The Hanafi school classifies kinayat into two categories: three specific words that, when intended, produce one revocable divorce, and all others that produce one irrevocable divorce. Understanding this classification is essential for determining the legal consequences of a husband's ambiguous statements.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two categories of kinayat
Identify the three special words (i'tiddi, istibri'i rahimak, anti wahidah) and understand they yield raj'i divorce. All others yield ba'in divorce.
This is a rule-based distinction rooted in customary usage.
2
Apply the condition of intention (niyyah)
For kinayat, divorce does not occur unless the speaker intended divorce or a contextual indication (dalalah hal) is present.
Contextual indication may substitute for explicit intention.
3
Distinguish between revocable and irrevocable divorce
The three special words produce raj'i divorce, allowing reconciliation during iddah. Other kinayat produce ba'in divorce, requiring a new marriage to reunite.
The consequence affects the couple's ability to reconcile.
4
Handle contradictory statements and intra-Hanafi disagreement
Understand the dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding repeated kinayat phrases like 'Anti taliq anti'.
The mufta bihi is to follow Abu Yusuf's view (one divorce), but the text does not explicitly settle it.

The second type: allusory words (kinayat). Divorce does not occur by them except with intention or contextual indication. They are of two types: among them are three words by which revocable divorce occurs, and only one divorce occurs by them, and they are: his saying: 'Observe your waiting period' and 'Declare your womb free' and 'You are one.' If he intended three, it would be three. If he said: 'You are divorc[؟]' (with incomplete word), it does not occur except with intention, except in the context of discussing divorce. If he said: 'O divorc' with kasra on the lam, divorce occurs even if he did not intend it. If he said: 'You are divorced, divorced' or said: 'You are divorced, you are divorced' and said: 'I meant the first,' he is believed religiously. Likewise if he said: 'I have divorced you, I have divorced you' or 'You are divorced, I have divorced you' or said: 'You are divorced,' and a man asked: 'What did you say?' He said: 'I have divorced her' or said: 'I said: she is divorced,' then it is one in court. And if he said to a woman who had been consummated: 'You are divorced, you' or 'You are divorced and you': Abu Yusuf said: one occurs, Muhammad said: two. * His saying: 'The second type: allusory words, and divorce does not occur by them except with intention or contextual indication; because they are ambiguous between divorce and other things, so intention or indication is necessary.' His saying: 'They are of two types: among them are three words by which revocable divorce occurs, and only one occurs by them, and they are: his saying: Observe your waiting period, and Declare your womb free, and You are one.' And the rest of the allusory words, if he intended divorce by them, become one irrevocable divorce. As for his saying: 'Observe your waiting period': because it could mean observing the waiting period from marriage or observing the blessings of God, so it needs intention. And his saying: 'Declare your womb free': it could mean 'because I have divorced you' or 'I intend your divorce.' And his saying: 'You are one' could be an adjective for an omitted noun, i.e. one divorce, and could mean 'you are one among your people.' And the declension of 'one' is not considered according to the majority of scholars, and this is correct because commoners do not distinguish between aspects of declension. And some said: if he places 'one' in the accusative, divorce occurs whether he intended or not; if in the nominative, nothing occurs even if he intended. And if he makes it sukun, there is discussion. The correct view is that all are the same in that it does not occur except with intention. * His saying: 'And the rest of the allusory words, if he intended divorce by them, become one irrevocable divorce.' All allusory words are irrevocable, except the three we mentioned. And al-Shafi'i said: all of them are revocable.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, explaining the second type of divorce expressions: allusory words (kinayat). The author begins by stating the general rule: divorce does not occur by kinayat unless accompanied by intention (niyyah) or a contextual indication (dalalah hal). This is because kinayat are ambiguous; they can mean divorce or something else. Next, he divides kinayat into two categories: three specific words that always yield one revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) when intended, and the rest that yield one irrevocable divorce (talaq ba'in) when intended. The three words are: 'I'tiddi' (observe your waiting period), 'Istibri'i rahimak' (declare your womb free), and 'Anti wahidah' (you are one). He then explains that if the husband intended three divorces with these words, it counts as three. Following, he discusses irregular pronunciations: saying 'Anti tali' with incomplete word does not divorce without intention except in a context of discussing divorce; saying 'Ya tali' with kasra on the lam effects divorce even without intention. He then covers repetition: if a man says 'Anti taliq taliq' or similar and claims he meant the first, he is believed religiously but not judicially. He also details a case where after pronouncing divorce, the husband's response to a question confirms one divorce; the court rules it as one. Afterwards, he presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement: if a man says to his consummated wife 'Anti taliq anti' or 'Anti taliq wa anti', Abu Yusuf rules one divorce occurs, while Imam Muhammad rules two. The commentary then explains the reasoning behind the three special words: each has multiple possible meanings, necessitating intention. The author also discusses the declension of 'wahidah' (one): the correct view is that declension does not affect the ruling; intention is always required. Finally, he reiterates that all kinayat except the three are ba'in, noting that Imam al-Shafi'i disagreed, considering all kinayat as raj'i.

Key Terms
الكنايات (al-kinayat): Words that allude to divorce but are not explicit, such as 'Observe your waiting period' or 'You are one.'
الطلاق الرجعي (al-talaq al-raj'i): Revocable divorce; the husband may take his wife back during the waiting period without a new marriage.
الطلاق البائن (al-talaq al-ba'in): Irrevocable divorce; the couple cannot reunite without a new marriage contract.
دلالة حال (dalalat al-hal): Contextual indication that clarifies the speaker's intention, such as being in a divorce discussion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 'I'tiddi'
C) 'Anti haram'
D) 'Al-salih'

Key Takeaway
Allusory divorce words require intention or context; three specific words yield revocable divorce, while all others yield irrevocable divorce.

Intention of Number in Kinaya Divorce: The Hanafi-Zufar Dispute
Commentary on rulings of kinaya divorce with intention of number

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why intending two divorces with a kinayah word results in only one according to Abu Hanifa, and evaluate Zufar's counter-argument.

Core Idea
A kinayah word does not carry number; intending two divorces yields one because baynunah is a type, not a count.

In this passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayra, the commentator explains a key rule in kinayah divorce: the intention of number is limited by the nature of the word. While intending three divorces is effective because it represents a type of severe separation, intending two is invalid because the word 'bā’in' cannot be made dual. Imam Zufar disagreed, arguing that intention can specify number. This lesson unpacks the proof and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested ruling
What is the rule about intending two divorces with a kinayah word? Who disagrees? The passage states that if a husband intends two, only one occurs. Zufar holds that two occur.
2
Trace the Hanafi proof
Why does baynunah not entail number? The Hanafi proof is linguistic: you cannot say 'you are two bā’ins' (anti bā’inatān). Intention cannot impose a meaning that the expression does not support.
3
Examine Zufar's objection and the Hanafi response
Zufar argued that intention can specify number since the word is ambiguous. The Hanafi response: number is not a property of the word; triple divorce is a type of separation, not a numerical count. For a slave wife, intending two equals the maximum severe separation, so it takes effect.
4
Apply the exception
How does the rule differ for a slave wife? If the husband tells his slave wife 'anti bā’in' intending two, two divorces occur because the highest baynunah for a slave is two, analogous to three for a free woman.

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayra comments on the rulings of kināyah (ambiguous) words of divorce. The commentary first addresses the rule that if a husband uses a kināyah word intending three divorces, three occur; if he intends two, only one occurs. The proof is that baynūnah (separation) does not entail a specific number—one does not say 'you are two separated ones'—so the intended number cannot alter the meaning unless it is a type of baynūnah (like triple separation for a free woman, or double for a slave woman). Zufar disagrees, arguing that two can occur by intention. The Hanafi response is that since the word itself does not carry a number, the intention of two is ineffective; only the nature of separation (severe vs. light) matters. The text then lists many words that are ambiguous (kināyah) and require intention: bā’in, battah, harām, etc. For each, it explains the possible meanings—divorce versus other interpretations—so that the husband's intention determines whether divorce occurs. The commentary emphasizes that intention is indispensable for these words. Finally, it mentions a specific ruling from Abū Ḥanīfah on the phrase 'I gifted you to your family' etc., stating it is divorce only if intended, because such phrases can also mean gifting the physical person. The passage ends mid-sentence discussing the case of gifting to a non-relative.

Key Terms
بائن (bā’in): A term meaning 'separated', used as kināyah for divorce; ambiguous between divorce and other separation (e.g., from religion).
بينونة (baynūnah): Separation; the state of being divorced. It can be light (with possibility of return) or severe (irrevocable).
كناية (kināyah): An ambiguous word that can mean divorce or something else; requires intention (niyyah) to effect divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, kinayah divorce words do not carry a numerical count; intending two only gives one unless the wife is a slave, where two is the maximum severe separation.

Kinayah in Talaq: The Necessity of Intention and Common Indirect Phrases
Enumeration of kinayah expressions with explanation of their ambiguity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify kinayah expressions in talaq and apply the Hanafi rule that intention is required for divorce to take effect.

Core Idea
An indirect divorce expression does not effect divorce unless the husband intends it.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the matn's principle that kinayah expressions require intention (niyyah). He then enumerates many common indirect phrases—like 'anti bā'in' and 'anti ḥarām'—and explains why each is ambiguous. The lesson equips you to recognize these expressions and understand the legal operation: divorce occurs only when the husband's intent is established.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The author begins with the foundational rule: kinayah requires intention. State this rule clearly.
2
Recognize the ambiguity
For each expression listed, note the multiple possible meanings (divorce vs. other interpretations). This step builds awareness of why intention is necessary.
3
Apply the rule
Given any kinayah expression, determine that divorce is valid only if the husband's intention to divorce can be proven.
4
Note the specific case of 'wahabtuki'
Examine the special rule from Abu Hanifa: 'wahabtuki' addressed to certain relatives is divorce if intended, but not for other relatives. Understand the rationale: she is returned by divorce to those relatives.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن هذه الألفاظ تحتمل الطلاق وغيره، فلا بد من النية. فقوله : أنتِ بائن: يحتمل البينونة من النكاح، ويحتمل من الدين. وقوله : وبتّةٌ : البت: هو القطع، فيحتمل القطع من النكاح، وعن المروءة والخير. - وبثلة : بمنزلة : بتة. وقوله : حرام: يحتمل الطلاق واليمين. وحبْلُك على غاربك: يحتمل لأنك قد بنتِ مني، ويحتمل أنك لا تطيعيني. والحقي بأهلك: يحتمل لأني طلقتك، ويحتمل الزيارة لأهلها. وخلية يحتمل من النكاح، ومن الخير، ومن الشغل. وبريَّةٌ : يحتمل من النكاح، ومن الدين. وقوله : ووهبتك لأهلك، سواء قبلوها أو لم يقبلوها: يحتمل وهبتك لهم ؛ لأنك قد بنت مني، ويحتمل هبة العين. وعن أبي حنيفة: إذا قال وهبتك لأهلك، أو لأبيك، أو لأمك، أو للأزواج : فهو طلاق إذا نوى؛ لأنها تُردُّ بالطلاق على هؤلاء، ويملكها الأزواج بعد الطلاق. وإذا قال: وهبتك لأخيك أو لعمك أو لخالك أو لفلان الأجنبي: لم يكن طلاقاً؛ لأنها لا تُردُّ بالطلاق على هؤلاء. وقوله : وسرحتك، وفارقتك : هما كنايتان عندنا؛ لأنهما يُستعملان في الطلاق وغيره. يقال: سرحتُ إبلي، وفارقتُ صديقي، فقوله: سرحتك: يحتمل بالطلاق، ويحتمل في حوائجي. وفارقتك : يحتمل الطلاق، ويحتمل ببدني. وقوله : وأنت حرة: يفيد التحريم، ويحتمل كونها حرة. وقوله : وتقنَّعي : يحتمل لأنك مطلقة، ويحتمل ستر العورة. ومثله: واستتري. وقوله : واغربي: يحتمل لأنك قد بِنْتِ مني، ويحتمل أنك لا تطيعيني. ومثله : اعزبي بالعين المهملة والزاي: ومعناه غيبي وابعدي، ومنه قوله تعالى: وَمَا يَعْزُبُ عَن رَّبِّكَ مِن مِثْقَالِ ذَرَّةٍ. يونس/ ٦١، والعزوب: البعد والذهاب. وقوله : وابتغي الأزواج يحتمل لأني طلقتك، ويحتمل إبعادها منه. ومن الكنايات أيضاً اخرجي واذهبي، وقومي، وتزوجي، وانطلقي، وانتقلي، ولا نكاح بيني وبينك، ولا سبيل لي عليك، ولا نكاح لي عليك.
Because these expressions are ambiguous between divorce and other meanings, intention is necessary. So his saying: 'You are ba'in' (bā'in) is ambiguous between separation from marriage and separation from religion. And his saying: 'battah' (battah): al-batt is cutting, so it is ambiguous between cutting from marriage and from manliness and goodness. 'bathlah' is like 'battah'. And his saying: 'haram' (ḥarām) is ambiguous between divorce and an oath. 'Your rope is on your neck' (ḥabluki 'alā ghāribik) is ambiguous between 'you have separated from me' and 'you will not obey me'. 'Go to your family' (alḥaqī bi-ahlik) is ambiguous between 'I have divorced you' and 'visiting her family'. 'khalīyah' (khalīyah) is ambiguous between separation from marriage, from goodness, and from occupation. 'barīyyah' (barīyyah) is ambiguous between separation from marriage and from religion. And his saying: 'I gift you to your family' (wahabtuki li-ahlik), whether they accept or not, is ambiguous between 'I gift you to them because you have separated from me' and 'gift of a physical object'. And from Abū Ḥanīfah: if he says 'I gift you to your family, or to your father, or to your mother, or to the husbands' then it is divorce if he intended, because she is returned through divorce to these, and the husbands possess her after divorce. And if he says 'I gift you to your brother, or to your uncle, or to your maternal uncle, or to so-and-so a stranger' then it is not divorce, because she is not returned through divorce to these. And his saying: 'I released you' (sarraḥtuk) and 'I separated from you' (fāraqtuk) are both kināyah according to us because they are used in divorce and other contexts. It is said: 'I released my camels' and 'I parted from my friend'. So his saying 'sarraḥtuk' is ambiguous between divorce and 'in my needs'. And 'fāraqtuk' is ambiguous between divorce and 'with my body'. And his saying: 'You are free' (anti ḥurrah) implies prohibition (al-taḥrīm) and is ambiguous between meaning she is free (from slavery). And his saying: 'Cover yourself' (taqanna‘ī) is ambiguous between 'because you are divorced' and 'covering the private parts'. Similarly: 'istatirī'. And his saying: 'Depart' (ughrubī) is ambiguous between 'you have separated from me' and 'you will not obey me'. Similarly: 'i‘zabī' with the unpointed 'ayn and zāy, meaning disappear and go away. And from it is His statement: 'And there does not escape from your Lord [the weight of] an atom' (Yūnus 61). And al-‘uzūb is distance and departure. And his saying: 'Seek husbands' (ibtaghī al-azwāj) is ambiguous between 'I have divorced you' and 'removing her from him'. And among kināyah also: 'exit' (ukhrujī), 'go' (idhhabī), 'stand' (qūmī), 'marry' (tazawwajī), 'go away' (inṭaliqī), 'move' (intaqilī), 'there is no marriage between me and you', 'there is no way for me over you', 'there is no marriage for me over you'.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage from the sharh expands on the matn's treatment of kināyah (indirect) expressions in divorce. The author states the key principle: because these words have multiple possible meanings (divorce or something else), intention (niyyah) is necessary for divorce to occur. He then lists numerous phrases, explaining each one's ambiguity. For example, 'anti bā'in' could mean separation from marriage or from religion; 'battah' (cutting) could mean cutting from marriage or from manliness; 'ḥarām' could mean divorce or an oath (as in a vow of abstention). The phrase 'ḥabluki 'alā ghāribik' (your rope on your neck) is literally about a camel's halter, metaphorically meaning freedom or disobedience. The commentary includes a specific ruling from Abu Hanifa about 'wahabtuki li-ahlik' (I gift you to your family): if addressed to the wife's family, father, mother, or husband(s), it is divorce if intended, because she is returned to them by divorce. But if addressed to a brother, uncle, or stranger, it is not divorce because she does not return to such persons by divorce. The passage continues with other common kināyah phrases like 'sarrahtuk' (I released you), 'fāraqtuk' (I separated from you), 'anti ḥurrah' (you are free), 'taqanna‘ī' (cover yourself), 'ughrubī' (depart), and 'i‘zabī' (go away, citing Quran 10:61 for the root meaning). It concludes with additional kināyah examples: 'ukhrujī' (exit), 'idhhabī' (go), and the negations of marriage. The underlying legal reasoning is based on the principle that words must be taken in their literal sense unless context or intention specifies otherwise. In Hanafi fiqh, these indefinite expressions do not effect divorce without evidence of intent. This passage is a practical guide for judges and scholars to identify which expressions require investigation into the husband's intention.

Key Terms
كناية (kināyah): An indirect expression that could imply divorce but has other possible meanings; it does not effect divorce without intention (niyyah).
بائن (bā'in): Here, a kinayah expression meaning 'separated' – ambiguous between separation from marriage and other separations (e.g., religion).
بتة (battah): Literally 'cutting'; as a kinayah, it may mean cutting the marriage tie or cutting from goodness/manliness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a husband says to his wife 'wahabtuki li-akhīk' (I gift you to your brother), does this count as divorce with intention?
A) Yes, because 'wahabtuki' is always a kinayah for divorce.
B) No, because the brother is not among those to whom she is returned by divorce.
C) Yes, if the wife does not accept the gift.
D) No, because it is a clear (sarih) expression.

Key Takeaway
Kinayah expressions in talaq require intention to divorce; without it, no divorce takes effect.

Kināyāt Words in Divorce Require Intention
List of allusive divorce words with ruling that they require intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a divorce word is a kināyah (allusive) or a ṣarīḥ (explicit) and state the Hanafi rule that kināyāt require intention for divorce.

Core Idea
A kināyah does not divorce without intention—words like 'go away' or 'cover yourself' need the husband's inner intent.

Not every word that sounds like separation actually counts as divorce. In Hanafi fiqh, a clear pronouncement like 'You are divorced' is direct, but words that can also mean other things are called kināyāt—allusive expressions. The great Hanafi jurists have carefully listed these words and established the rule: without a specific intention of divorce, they have no legal effect. This lesson walks through the key kināyāt and the principle that governs them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the kināyah pair
For each word listed, note both its divorce and non-divorce usage as given in the text. For example, 'سرحتك' (I released you) can also mean releasing camels.
This step helps you see why the word is allusive.
2
Apply the Hanafi rule
For each word, state that divorce occurs only if the husband intended divorce when uttering it. The text emphasizes this at the end: 'If he intended by it divorce, it is divorce; otherwise, not.'
The rule is uniform for all kināyāt.
3
Expand with additional examples
The commentator lists more words like 'اخرجي' (exit), 'اذهبي' (go), 'قومي' (stand up), etc. Recognize them as kināyāt and apply the same rule.
The list is not exhaustive; the principle applies to any word that has both divorce and non-divorce meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : وسرحتك، وفارقتك : هما كنايتان عندنا؛ لأنهما يُستعملان في الطلاق وغيره.
يقال: سرحتُ إبلي، وفارقتُ صديقي، فقوله: سرحتك: يحتمل بالطلاق، ويحتمل في حوائجي.
وفارقتك : يحتمل الطلاق، ويحتمل ببدني.
وقوله : وأنت حرة: يفيد التحريم، ويحتمل كونها حرة.
وقوله : وتقنَّعي : يحتمل لأنك مطلقة، ويحتمل ستر العورة.
ومثله: واستتري.
وقوله : واغربي: يحتمل لأنك قد بِنْتِ مني، ويحتمل أنك لا تطيعيني.
ومثله : اعزبي بالعين المهملة والزاي: ومعناه غيبي وابعدي، ومنه قوله تعالى: وَمَا يَعْزُبُ عَن رَّبِّكَ مِن مِثْقَالِ ذَرَّةٍ. يونس/ ٦١، والعزوب: البعد والذهاب.
وقوله : وابتغي الأزواج يحتمل لأني طلقتك، ويحتمل إبعادها منه. ومن الكنايات أيضاً اخرجي واذهبي، وقومي، وتزوجي، وانطلقي، وانتقلي، ولا نكاح بيني وبينك، ولا سبيل لي عليك، ولا نكاح لي عليك.
فإن أراد به الطلاق كان طلاقاً، وإلا فلا.
And his statement: 'I released you' and 'I separated from you': both are allusive words (kināyāt) according to us because they are used for divorce and other things. It is said: I released my camels, and I separated from my friend. So his statement 'I released you' probabilistically means divorce, and may mean regarding my affairs. 'I separated from you' probabilistically means divorce, and may mean with my body. And his statement 'You are free' indicates prohibition, and may mean she is free (not a slave). And his statement 'Cover yourself' probabilistically means because you are divorced, and may mean covering the nakedness. Similarly, 'Veil yourself'. And his statement 'Go away' probabilistically means because you have become separated from me, and may mean that you do not obey me. Similarly, 'Depart' with the unpointed 'ayn and zay, meaning be absent and go far, and from it is the statement of Allah Most High: 'And not absent from your Lord is the weight of an atom' (Yunus 61). And al-uzub means distance and going. And his statement 'Seek husbands' probabilistically means because I have divorced you, and may mean to distance her from him. Also from the allusive words are: 'Exit', 'Go', 'Stand up', 'Marry', 'Depaet', 'Move', 'No marriage between me and you', 'I have no way over you', and 'I have no marriage over you.' If he intended by it divorce, it is divorce; otherwise, not.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage is an expansion from the commentator on the Quduri matn, listing various allusive words (kināyāt) used in divorce. The key principle is that kināyāt are words that have both a literal and a commonly understood divorce meaning but are also used in other contexts. According to the Hanafi school, such words do not effect divorce unless the husband intends divorce at the time of utterance. The commentator provides examples: 'سرحتك' (I released you) and 'فارقتك' (I separated from you) are both kināyāt because they are used for camels and friends respectively. Other examples include 'وأنت حرة' (you are free) which could mean she is a free woman, 'وتقنعي' (cover yourself) could mean to cover for modesty, 'واغربي' (go away) could mean she is disobedient, etc. The verse from the Quran is cited to show the root meaning of 'اعزبي' (depart). The passage concludes that if the husband intended divorce with any of these words, then divorce occurs; otherwise, it does not. This is a fundamental ruling in Hanafi fiqh on indirect expressions.

Key Terms
كناية (kināyah): A word that is used both in divorce and non-divorce contexts; it does not effect divorce without intention.
طلاق (ṭalāq): Dissolution of marriage by the husband's pronouncement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Kināyāt words in divorce require the husband's intention; without it, no divorce occurs.

Rulings on Barā'ah, Command, and Invocation Expressions in Divorce
Four specific divorce-related expressions and their rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings for the four expressions and identify the conditions that trigger or prevent talaq in each case.

Core Idea
Intent distinguishes valid divorce from ineffective words; a command requires acceptance; an invocation suffices without intent.

This lesson examines four specific expressions a husband might use in the context of divorce. The Hanafi school analyses each to determine whether a valid talaq occurs and under what conditions. Understanding these nuances protects against unintended divorces and clarifies the role of intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression type
Classify each expression as kināyah (requires intention) or ṣarīḥ (explicit, does not require intention). Note: barā'ah from marriage is kināyah; barā'ah from divorce is contradictory; command and invocation are treated specially.
2
Determine the need for intention
For barā'ah from marriage, intention is required; for invocation, intention is not required; for command, the wife's acceptance triggers talaq, but the offer itself shows intent.
The text states 'whether he intended or not' for invocation, indicating it is ṣarīḥ.
3
Apply the ruling to each scenario
For each expression, state whether talaq occurs and under what conditions: barā'ah from marriage occurs with intent; barā'ah from divorce never occurs; 'Take your divorce' occurs upon wife's acceptance; 'May Allah divorce you' occurs regardless of intent.
4
Reason from the underlying principle
Abandonment of marriage implies divorce; abandonment of divorce is impossible; a command to take divorce is an offer that can be accepted; a prayer for divorce is understood as a direct pronouncement due to cultural usage.
This reasoning explains why the rulings differ.
5
Compare with other schools if applicable
Some schools consider 'May Allah divorce you' as a prayer not a divorce, but Hanafi treats it as ṣarīḥ. Recognize the practical consequence of the ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال: أنا بريء من نكاحك وقع الطلاق إذا نواه.
وإن قال: أنا بريء من طلاقك: لا يقع شيء؛ لأن البراءة من الشيء : تَرْكُ له، وإعراض عنه، والمُعرِض عن الطلاق: لا يكون مطلقاً، والمعرض عن النكاح يكون مطلقاً. كذا في «الواقعات».
ولو قال: خذي طلاقك، فقالت: قد أخذتُه : طَلقت.
ولو قال لها: طلقك الله، أو قال لأمته: أعتقك الله: وقع الطلاق والعتاق نوى أو لم ينو.
And if he said: 'I am free from your marriage', divorce occurs if he intended it. If he said: 'I am free from your divorce', nothing occurs because freedom from a thing is leaving it and turning away from it, and the one turning away from divorce is not a divorcer, and the one turning away from marriage is a divorcer. This is in al-Waqi'at. And if he said: 'Take your divorce', and she said: 'I have taken it', she is divorced. And if he said to her: 'May Allah divorce you', or said to his slave girl: 'May Allah free you', divorce and manumission occur whether he intended or not.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage presents four distinct rulings. First, 'أنا بريء من نكاحك' (I am free from your marriage) is a kināyah that requires intention to effect divorce; the reasoning is that being free from something implies abandonment, and in the context of marriage, abandonment of marriage is divorce. Second, 'أنا بريء من طلاقك' (I am free from your divorce) does not effect anything because one cannot be free from divorce itself—it is a contradictory expression. Third, 'خذي طلاقك' (take your divorce) is a command; when the wife accepts by saying 'I have taken it', divorce occurs because the husband's offer and the wife's acceptance form a kind of assignment. Fourth, 'طلقك الله' (May Allah divorce you) and 'أعتقك الله' (May Allah free you) are considered as direct invocations that effect divorce and manumission even without the husband's specific intention, because they are understood as pronouncements in the form of a prayer. The phrase 'نوى أو لم ينو' (whether he intended or not) indicates that these are ṣarīḥ (explicit) words according to some, or treated as such. Note that for the slave girl, it's about manumission, not divorce.

Key Terms
البراءة (al-barā'ah): Freedom from something; in the context, 'براءة من النكاح' means abandonment of marriage, implying divorce.
طلقك الله (ṭallaqaka Allāh): May Allah divorce you; a formula that effects divorce without intention because it is understood as a direct pronouncement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a husband says to his wife 'Take your divorce' and she says 'I have taken it'?
A) She is not divorced until he says 'I divorce you'.
B) She is divorced upon her acceptance.
C) It depends on whether witnesses are present.
D) It is invalid because the wording is not clear.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the effect of divorce expressions depends on the form: kināyah requires intention, command requires acceptance, and invocation is effective without intention.

General Statements in Divorce and Manumission
Rulings on general divorce and manumission statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a general statement includes one's wife in divorce and distinguish between the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on freeing one's own slave via general manumission.

Core Idea
A general statement includes one's wife in divorce, but freeing slaves by general statement is disputed between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

This lesson examines the legal effect of sweeping pronouncements like 'All women are divorced' or 'All slaves are free.' The Hanafi school holds that such general statements do include one's own wife or slave, but there is an intra-madhhab difference regarding manumission. Additionally, hyperbolic expressions like 'all children of Adam are free' are not taken literally by consensus, preserving the slave's status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Statement and Its Scope
Read the statement: Is it a general claim about all women, all slaves, or all of humanity? Determine whether one's own wife or slave falls under the wording.
General words (ʿumūm) are taken literally unless there is a clear exception.
2
Apply the Rule on Divorce by Umum
If the husband says 'All women of the world are divorced,' his wife is divorced by the general expression. The court does not accept his claim that he did not intend her.
The general term is binding; no specific intention is required in court.
3
Analyze the Manumission Ikhtilaf
For 'the slaves of the people of the world are free,' Abu Yusuf says his own slave is not freed (because the statement is too general and lacks specific intention), while Muhammad says he is freed (the general word includes his slave).
Both are Hanafi positions; the difference hinges on the role of intention in general manumission.
4
Recognize the Consensus Exception
If he says 'All children of Adam are free,' his slave is not freed by consensus. Such hyperbolic speech is not intended literally.
Hyperbole and exaggeration override the general wording when the context clearly indicates non-literalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال: جميع نساء الدنيا طوالق تطلق امرأته، ولا يصدق في القضاء أنه لم ينوها.
-
وإن قال : عبيد أهل الدنيا أحرار
قال أبو يوسف : لا يعتق عبده، وقال محمد: يعتق.
ولو قال: أولاد آدم كلهم أحرار لا يعتق عبده، إجماعاً. كذا في «الواقعات».
ولو قال: لست لي بامرأة، أو قال ما أنت لي بامرأة : كان طلاقاً عند أبي حنيفة.
وكذا : ما أنا بزوجك.
And if he said: 'All the women of the world are divorced', his wife is divorced, and he is not believed in court that he did not intend her. And if he said: 'The slaves of the people of the world are free', Abu Yusuf said: his slave is not freed, and Muhammad said: he is freed. And if he said: 'All the children of Adam are free', his slave is not freed by consensus. This is in al-Waqi'at. And if he said: 'You are not my wife', or said 'You are not a wife to me', it is divorce according to Abu Hanifa. Similarly: 'I am not your husband'.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage addresses several scenarios involving generalized statements. The first ruling: if a husband says 'All women of the world are divorced,' his wife is included and thus divorced. The court does not accept his claim that he did not intend his wife because the statement is all-encompassing. The second ruling involves a similar statement about freeing slaves. Here, Abu Yusuf holds that the owner's slave is not freed because the statement is too general and may not specifically include his own slave, while Imam Muhammad holds that it does include his slave and thus he is freed. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The third ruling: if he says 'All children of Adam are free,' there is consensus (ijmāʿ) that his slave is not freed, because the expression is hyperbolic and not meant literally. Finally, the passage mentions explicit negations: saying 'You are not my wife' or 'I am not your husband' is considered divorce according to Abu Hanifa. These are treated as ṣarīḥ (explicit) words of divorce because they directly negate the marital relationship.

Key Terms
الطلاق بالعموم (al-ṭalāq bi-al-ʿumūm): Divorce by a general statement that includes one's wife.
العتاق بالعموم (al-ʿatāq bi-al-ʿumūm): Manumission by a general statement that includes one's slave.
الإجماع (al-ijmāʿ): Consensus; here, consensus that saying 'all children of Adam are free' does not free one's slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says 'All women of the world are divorced,' according to the Hanafi school, his wife is divorced because:
A) He intended her specifically.
B) The general statement includes her.
C) He must have meant it.
D) Consensus requires it.

Key Takeaway
General statements about divorce include one's wife; for manumission, there is intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf requires specific intent, Muhammad does not.

Explicit Negation as Divorce: 'You Are Not My Wife'
Rule on explicit negation as divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify explicit negation phrases that constitute divorce according to Abu Hanifa and distinguish them from kinayah expressions.

Core Idea
Saying 'You are not my wife' or 'I am not your husband' is divorce—no intention needed.

The passage concludes with a ruling on direct negation statements that deny the marital relationship itself. According to Abu Hanifa, phrases like 'You are not my wife' or 'I am not your husband' are considered explicit (ṣarīḥ) words of divorce, taking effect immediately regardless of the speaker's intention. This lesson explores why these negations are treated as talaq and how they differ from indirect kinayah expressions.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the Phrase as a Direct Negation of Marriage
Identify statements that explicitly deny the existence of the marital bond, such as 'You are not my wife' or 'I am not your husband.'
These are categorical negations, not conditions or future statements.
2
Apply Abu Hanifa's Rule: Such Negation is Ṣarīḥ Talaq
According to Abu Hanifa, these phrases are treated as explicit divorce. The court does not require proof of intention; the words themselves effect one divorce.
This is because the negation directly contradicts the essence of marriage, which is equivalent to dissolving it.
3
Compare with Kinayah Expressions
Contrast with indirect phrases like 'You are free' or 'Go to your family,' which require intention. Explicit negations need no intention because they are ṣarīḥ (clear).
The clarity of the negation makes it similar to uttering 'talaq' explicitly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال: جميع نساء الدنيا طوالق تطلق امرأته، ولا يصدق في القضاء أنه لم ينوها.
-
وإن قال : عبيد أهل الدنيا أحرار
قال أبو يوسف : لا يعتق عبده، وقال محمد: يعتق.
ولو قال: أولاد آدم كلهم أحرار لا يعتق عبده، إجماعاً. كذا في «الواقعات».
ولو قال: لست لي بامرأة، أو قال ما أنت لي بامرأة : كان طلاقاً عند أبي حنيفة.
وكذا : ما أنا بزوجك.
And if he said: 'All the women of the world are divorced', his wife is divorced, and he is not believed in court that he did not intend her. And if he said: 'The slaves of the people of the world are free', Abu Yusuf said: his slave is not freed, and Muhammad said: he is freed. And if he said: 'All the children of Adam are free', his slave is not freed by consensus. This is in al-Waqi'at. And if he said: 'You are not my wife', or said 'You are not a wife to me', it is divorce according to Abu Hanifa. Similarly: 'I am not your husband'.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage addresses several scenarios involving generalized statements. The first ruling: if a husband says 'All women of the world are divorced,' his wife is included and thus divorced. The court does not accept his claim that he did not intend his wife because the statement is all-encompassing. The second ruling involves a similar statement about freeing slaves. Here, Abu Yusuf holds that the owner's slave is not freed because the statement is too general and may not specifically include his own slave, while Imam Muhammad holds that it does include his slave and thus he is freed. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The third ruling: if he says 'All children of Adam are free,' there is consensus (ijmāʿ) that his slave is not freed, because the expression is hyperbolic and not meant literally. Finally, the passage mentions explicit negations: saying 'You are not my wife' or 'I am not your husband' is considered divorce according to Abu Hanifa. These are treated as ṣarīḥ (explicit) words of divorce because they directly negate the marital relationship.

Key Terms
الصريح (al-ṣarīḥ): Explicit words of divorce that do not require intention.
الكناية (al-kināyah): Indirect expressions that may imply divorce but require intention.

D) It is a lie but not divorce.

Key Takeaway
Explicit negation of marriage is divorce according to Abu Hanifa; the speaker's intention is irrelevant.

Rulings on Phrases of Disassociation (Barā'ah)
Ruling on phrases of disassociation in divorce context

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the phrase 'I am free from your marriage' and identify when it constitutes divorce.

Core Idea
Saying 'I am free from your marriage' is a kināyah for divorce that requires intention; saying 'I am free from your divorce' has no effect.

The passage from Al-Jawharat al-Nayirah clarifies the legal effect of two common phrases of disassociation in marriage. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying kināyah expressions in divorce. This lesson will help you distinguish between phrases that can be divorce by intention and those that are legally irrelevant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Identify what the husband says and whether it constitutes divorce according to the passage. Note the conditional nature of the ruling.
2
Understand the role of intention
Recognize that the phrase 'barī' min nikaḥik' is a kināyah, which requires intention (niyyah) to effect divorce. Without intention, no divorce occurs.
Intention is a condition for kināyah expressions.
3
Differentiate between two phrases
Compare 'barī' min nikaḥik' with 'barī' min ṭalāqik'. The latter never produces divorce even with intention because it means turning away from divorce itself.
The linguistic reasoning is key: turning away from divorce is not an act of divorce.
4
Note the source authority
The ruling is cited from al-Wāqi'āt, a collection of later Hanafi fatāwā, indicating its status as a relied-upon opinion.
This is from fatawā mutakhkhirin, not Zahir al-Riwayah, but still authoritative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أراد به الطلاق كان طلاقاً، وإلا فلا. ولو قال: أنا بريء من نكاحك وقع الطلاق إذا نواه. وإن قال: أنا بريء من طلاقك: لا يقع شيء؛ لأن البراءة من الشيء : تَرْكُ له، وإعراض عنه، والمُعرِض عن الطلاق: لا يكون مطلقاً، والمعرض عن النكاح يكون مطلقاً. كذا في «الواقعات».

Likewise in al-Wāqi'āt.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on phrases of disassociation (barā'ah). The author states that if a husband says 'I am free from your marriage' and intends divorce, it is a valid divorce. However, if he says 'I am free from your divorce', no legal effect occurs. The reasoning is that the word 'barī'' means turning away from something: turning away from divorce does not constitute an act of divorce, but turning away from marriage (by disavowing the marital bond) is considered a form of divorce. The passage is cited from al-Wāqi'āt, a later collection of fatāwā, indicating its authority as a relied-upon ruling.

Key Terms
بريء (barī'): Disassociation, turning away from something; used in the context of expressing freedom from a legal bond.
الواقعات (al-Wāqi'āt): A collection of later legal rulings and fatwās, often cited as authoritative in the Hanafi school.

Ruling on 'Take Your Divorce'
Ruling on ṣarīḥ divorce by command

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of saying 'take your divorce' and explain why the wife's acceptance is not a condition for the divorce to take effect.

Core Idea
A husband's explicit command 'take your divorce' effects divorce immediately; the wife's acceptance is confirmatory, not constitutive.

This brief passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyirah addresses a common verbal formula for divorce. When the husband says 'take your divorce', it is a ṣarīḥ (explicit) pronouncement. The wife's response merely acknowledges the divorce; it does not create it. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion about unilateral divorce in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance
Recognize that the husband's words 'خذي طلاقك' are an explicit command containing the word 'ṭalāq'.
This is a ṣarīḥ expression.
2
Classify the expression
In Hanafi law, ṣarīḥ expressions of divorce are effective even without the husband's intention (niyya).
Contrast with kināyah expressions which require intent.
3
Determine the legal effect
The text states 'طلقت' (she is divorced), meaning the divorce occurs immediately upon the husband's utterance.
The wife's statement 'قد أخذته' is not a condition for effectiveness.
4
Clarify the role of the wife's response
The wife's acceptance is merely confirmatory. It does not affect the occurrence of divorce.
If she remained silent or refused, the divorce would still take effect.

Source Text

Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This is a short ruling on direct verbal expression. When the husband explicitly says 'take your divorce' (a clear command using the word 'divorce') and the wife responds by accepting, the divorce takes effect immediately. The husband's statement is an īqā' (enactment) of divorce, and the wife's acceptance, while not necessary for validity in tālaq, confirms her acknowledgment, but the divorce occurs by the husband's utterance alone.

Ruling on 'Allāh Divorces You' and 'Allāh Frees You'
Ruling on delegating divorce or freeing to Allāh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband says 'May Allah divorce you' and when a master says 'May Allah free you', and explain why intention is irrelevant.

Core Idea
Invoking Allah's name in divorce or emancipation makes it effective regardless of intention.

This lesson examines a specific but important verbal formula in Hanafi family and slave law. The phrases 'Allāh divorces you' and 'Allāh frees you' are not treated as supplications but as direct enactments. Understanding this ruling clarifies the Hanafi approach to linguistic forms and the role of intention in oral declarations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pronouncements
The passage mentions two phrases: 'ṭallaqak Allāh' (May Allāh divorce you) addressed to a wife, and 'aʿtaqak Allāh' (May Allāh free you) addressed to a slave-girl.
These phrases attribute the action directly to Allāh.
2
Determine the legal effect
According to the text, both divorce and emancipation occur (يقع الطلاق والعتاق) upon uttering these phrases.
The effect is immediate and unconditional.
3
Examine the role of intention
The passage explicitly states 'نوى أو لم ينو' (whether he intended or not). Intention (niyya) is irrelevant for the validity of these pronouncements.
This is an exception to the general rule that contracts and declarations require intent.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The Hanafi school treats these phrases as enacting declarations (īqāʿ) because attributing the action to Allāh implies its occurrence as a divine decree, leaving no room for human uncertainty.
This is based on the certainty (qatʿ) of the Divine act.
5
Compare with other schools
Other schools (e.g., Shāfiʿī) may require intention or consider such phrases ineffective because they view them as supplications rather than direct enactments.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf) is whether the marriage or slavery continues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال لها: طلقك الله، أو قال لأمته: أعتقك الله: وقع الطلاق والعتاق نوى أو لم ينو.
And if he said to her: 'May Allāh divorce you', or said to his slave-girl: 'May Allāh free you', divorce and emancipation occur whether he intended or not.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage addresses the effect of invoking Allāh's name in divorce or manumission. When a husband says to his wife 'ṭallaqak Allāh' (May Allāh divorce you), it is considered an effective divorce even if he did not intend it, because the phrase attributes the act of divorce to Allāh in a manner that implies it is already decreed. Similarly, saying to one's slave-girl 'aʿtaqak Allāh' (May Allāh free you) results in her emancipation. This is based on the principle that such statements are treated as direct enactments (īqāʿ) due to the certainty of the Divine act, unlike human actions that require intent.

Key Terms
طلقك الله (ṭallaqak Allāh): An expression meaning 'May Allāh divorce you', treated as an effective divorce pronouncement in Hanafi law.
أعتقك الله (aʿtaqak Allāh): An expression meaning 'May Allāh free you', treated as effective manumission of a slave.

Ruling on a General Statement of Divorce
Ruling on a general statement divorcing all women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband makes a general statement of divorce involving all women.

Core Idea
A general statement of divorce includes your wife, regardless of your unspoken intent.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Hanafi fiqh regarding the legal effect of a hyperbolic statement of divorce. When a husband says 'all women are divorced,' the court treats this as an effective divorce of his wife, even if he later claims he did not intend her. We will explore the reasoning behind this strict application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act
The husband's utterance 'جميع نساء الدنيا طوالق' (all women of the world are divorced) is a verbal pronouncement of divorce.
2
Determine the Legal Consequence
His own wife is divorced (tallaqa) by this statement. The talaq takes effect immediately.
3
Analyse the Evidentiary Rule
If the husband claims in court that he did not intend his wife, his claim is not accepted (lā yusaddaq). The court upholds the divorce based on the apparent meaning.
This is due to the principle that a general statement applies to all its members, and his wife is undeniably part of 'all women'.
4
Understand the Basis
The ruling is based on the Hanafi maxim that a general statement ('āmm) is binding on all its instances unless a specific exclusion (takhṣīṣ) is proven. Since the statement is absolute and includes his wife, the divorce is valid.
Contrast with a statement like 'all women are divorced except my wife', which would exclude her.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال: جميع نساء الدنيا طوالق تطلق امرأته، ولا يصدق في القضاء أنه لم ينوها.
And if he said: 'All women of the world are divorced', his wife is divorced, and he is not believed in court that he did not intend her.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This ruling deals with a hyperbolic statement. When a husband says 'all women are divorced' (ṭawāliq, plural of ṭāliq = divorced), his own wife is included and thus divorced. Even if he claims he did not intend his wife, the court does not accept such a claim because the statement is general and his wife is part of the set. This is based on the legal maxim that a general statement is applied to its entirety unless there is evidence of exception. In this case, the husband's wife is undeniably among 'all women'.

Key Terms
جميع نساء الدنيا طوالق (jamīʿ nisāʾ al-dunyā ṭawāliq): A broad expression meaning 'all women of the world are divorced', used in legal context to include one's wife.
لا يصدق (lā yusaddaq): He is not believed (in court). Refers to the husband's claim of not intending his wife being rejected.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) His wife is divorced, and his claim is not accepted in court.
C) His wife is divorced only if he also says her name.

Key Takeaway

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on General Manumission: Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on manumission of a specific slave via general statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the manumission of one's own slave through a general statement.

Core Idea
A general statement about 'all slaves of the world' may or may not include one's own slave depending on interpretation.

This passage introduces a subtle disagreement within the Hanafi school regarding the scope of a general manumission declaration. When a master says 'all slaves of the people of the world are free,' does his own slave become free? The two senior students of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, gave conflicting answers, illustrating how general language can be interpreted differently within the same school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed element
The act is the verbal declaration: 'عبيد أهل الدنيا أحرار'. The dispute centers on whether this includes the speaker's own slave.
2
State Abu Yusuf's position and reasoning
Abu Yusuf holds that his own slave is not freed, presumably because 'ahl al-dunyā' refers to other people, not oneself.
The text does not give his explicit dalil, but the implication is a restrictive interpretation.
3
State Muhammad's position and reasoning
Muhammad holds that the slave is freed, taking the phrase as general and inclusive of the speaker's own slave.
Again, no dalil is provided, but the stance relies on the apparent generality of the wording.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Yusuf's view the slave remains in bondage; under Muhammad's view the slave becomes free immediately upon the utterance.
This affects the legal status of the slave and any subsequent transactions involving him.
5
Note the absence of Abu Hanifa's view and tarjih
The text does not mention Abu Hanifa's opinion nor does it indicate which view is mufta bihi (operative for fatwa). This is an unresolved ikhtilaf within the school.
In practice, later Hanafi jurists may have given preference, but this passage leaves it open.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قال : عبيد أهل الدنيا أحرار قال أبو يوسف : لا يعتق عبده، وقال محمد: يعتق.
And if he said: 'The slaves of the people of the world are free', Abu Yusuf said: his slave is not emancipated, and Muhammad said: he is emancipated.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding a general manumission statement. When a master says 'all slaves of the people of the world are free', does his own slave become free? Abu Yusuf holds that his slave is not freed because the statement refers to slaves of others (people of the world), not his own, unless specifically intended. Muhammad, however, holds that his slave is included because the phrase 'slaves of the people of the world' is general and encompasses his slave as well. The text does not mention Abu Hanifa's position here. Note that this is a dispute within the Hanafi school, and both rulings are recorded.

Key Terms

The Rule of 'All Children of Adam Are Free'
Ijma' ruling that a general statement about all humans does not free one's slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a master says 'All children of Adam are free' and explain why the consensus (ijma') prevents emancipation.

Core Idea
A statement covering all humanity does not free a specific slave by consensus due to extreme generality.

We saw earlier a dispute about 'slaves of the world'. Here the text presents an even broader statement: 'All children of Adam are free'. The Hanafi scholars are unanimous that this does not emancipate one's slave. Understanding this ruling clarifies how generality limits legal effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the statement and its ruling
Recognize that the statement is 'All children of Adam are free'. The ruling is that his slave is not emancipated, as stated in the text.
The ruling is unequivocal: 'لا يعتق عبده'.
2
Recognize the force of ijma'
The text says 'إجماعاً' (by consensus). This means there is no disagreement among Hanafi scholars on this point. Ijma' is a binding source of law.
Consensus settles any potential dispute.
3
Explain the ratio legis
The reason is that the statement is too general. It is understood as a general assertion of fact, not a deliberate act of manumission. Extreme generality negates legal effect.
Compare with more specific statements that might be valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال: أولاد آدم كلهم أحرار لا يعتق عبده، إجماعاً. كذا في «الواقعات».
And if he said: 'All children of Adam are free', his slave is not emancipated, by consensus. Likewise in al-Wāqi'āt.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage addresses a statement that is even more general: 'all children of Adam' – meaning all human beings. Despite the generality, the Hanafi scholars agree (ijmāʿ) that such a statement does not emancipate one's own slave. The reason is that the term 'children of Adam' is too broad and is not considered a valid manumission formula for a specific slave; it is understood as a general statement of belief or fact, not a deliberate act of emancipation. This contrasts with the previous dispute about 'slaves of the world', where there was disagreement. The consensus here shows that extreme generality negates any legal effect.

Key Terms
إجماعاً (ijmāʿan): By consensus; here referring to agreement among Hanafi scholars that a specific slave is not freed by such a general statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage
