Denial of Marriage as Divorce: Kināyah, Intention, and the Sahibayn's Objection
Intra-Hanafi dispute on nafy al-zawjiyyah as kināyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether nafy al-zawjiyyah constitutes divorce, apply the mudhākarat al-ṭalāq exception, and distinguish between qaḍāʾ and dīn.

Core Idea
Denying your wife is a valid divorce if intended or in a divorce discussion – but only in court without intention.

This passage presents a fascinating intra-Hanafi conflict. Abu Hanifa treats expressions like 'you are not my wife' as allusive (kināyah) that can effect divorce depending on intention and context. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) reject this entirely, arguing that denying an existing marriage is a lie which cannot produce a legal effect. The text also introduces a crucial principle: judicial rulings may differ from religious reality before Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested expressions
The passage lists phrases: 'لست لي بامرأة', 'ما أنت لي بامرأة', 'ما أنا بزوجك'. These are the kināyah phrases in dispute.
All are denials of the marital tie.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's ruling
For him, if the husband intends divorce, it is divorce. If no intention, it is not divorce, except if they are in a context of discussing divorce (mudhākarat al-ṭalāq). In that context, the court will treat it as divorce even without intention, but religiously it only counts if he intended.
The exception is based on the principle that the outward context indicates intention in court.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's objection
They say none of these are divorce, whether intended or not, because it is a lie (the marriage exists). They draw qiyas with saying 'I never married you' – that is a lie and does nothing.
Their argument is qiyas jaliy: the denial is a false statement, and false statements have no legal effect.
4
Examine the unanimous exception: oath
All three imams agree that if the denial is expressed with an oath (e.g., 'والله ما أنت لي بامرأة'), no divorce occurs even if intended. The oath makes it a report (khabar) about the past, and a report cannot enact divorce.
This is because oaths only emphasize news, and news cannot effect divorce; compare to saying 'I divorced you yesterday' when it is false.
5
Apply the qaḍāʾ vs. dīn distinction
In the mudhākarat context, the court enforces divorce even if the husband had no inner intention. But before Allah, it is not valid unless he intended. This separation is a hallmark of Hanafi fiqh.
Students must understand that judicial validity is not always identical to religious validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال: لست لي بامرأة، أو قال ما أنت لي بامرأة : كان طلاقاً عند أبي حنيفة. وكذا : ما أنا بزوجك. فإن لم تكن له نية الطلاق : لم يقع بهذه الألفاظ طلاق، إلا أن يكونا في مذاكرة الطلاق : فيقعُ بها الطلاق في القضاء، ولا يقع فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى، إلا أن ينوي به الطلاق . أو سئل هل لك امرأة؟ فقال : لا : فإنه إن نوى الطلاق كان طلاقاً عند أبي حنيفة. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : لا يكون شيء من ذلك طلاقاً، نوى أو لم ينو ؛ لأن نفي الزوجية كذب، فلا يقع به شيء، كقوله: لم أتزوجك. وقد اتفقوا جميعاً على أنه لو قال: والله ما أنتِ لي بامرأة، أو لستِ والله لي بامرأة : أنه لا يقع به شيء وإن نوى؛ لأن اليمين على النفي يتناول الماضي، وهو كاذب فيه، فلا يقع شيء. ولأنه لما أكَّد النفي باليمين : صار ذلك إخباراً، لا إيقاعاً؛ لأن اليمين لا يُؤكَّد بها إلا الخبر، والخبر لا يقع به الطلاق، ألا ترى أنه لو قال: كنت طلقتك أمس: لم يقع بذلك شيء إذا لم يكن طلقها أمس. كذا في شرحه». قوله: (فإن لم تكن له نيةٌ : لم يقع بهذه الألفاظ طلاق، إلا أن يكونا في مذاكرة الطلاق)، وهو أن تطالبه بالطلاق، أو تطالبه بطلاق غيرها. * قوله: (فيقعُ بها الطلاق في القضاء، ولا يقع فيما بينه وبين تعالى، إلا أن ينويه).
And if he said: 'You are not my wife', or said 'You are not a wife to me', it was divorce according to Abu Hanifa. And likewise: 'I am not your husband'. If he had no intention of divorce, divorce does not occur by these phrases, except if they are in a context of discussing divorce: then divorce occurs by them in court, but does not occur between him and Allah Almighty, unless he intended divorce. Or if he was asked 'Do you have a wife?' and he said 'No': then if he intended divorce, it is divorce according to Abu Hanifa. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: None of that is divorce, whether he intended or not, because negation of marriage is a lie, so nothing occurs by it, like his saying 'I never married you'. And they all agreed that if he said: 'By Allah, you are not my wife', or 'By Allah, you are not a wife to me', nothing occurs even if he intended, because the oath on negation pertains to the past, and he is lying in it, so nothing occurs. And because when he emphasized the negation with an oath, that becomes an informative statement, not an enacting one; because an oath is only used to emphasize news, and news does not cause divorce. Do you not see that if he said: 'I divorced you yesterday', nothing occurs by that if he had not divorced her yesterday. Likewise in his commentary. He said: (If he had no intention: divorce does not occur by these phrases, except if they are in a context of discussing divorce), which is that she demands divorce from him, or she demands divorce for another woman. * He said: (Then divorce occurs by them in court, and does not occur between him and Allah Almighty, unless he intended it).
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether phrases that deny the marital relationship (e.g., 'you are not my wife', 'I am not your husband') constitute divorce. Abu Hanifa holds that they are kināyah (allusive) expressions: if the husband intends divorce, it takes effect. If he does not intend, it does not occur, except in a context of discussing divorce (mudhākarat al-ṭalāq) – then the divorce is effective in court (qaḍā'an) but not religiously (bainahu wa bain Allāh) unless he genuinely intended it. The principle is that the outward context indicates an intention to divorce, but the inner reality is judged by Allāh. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree entirely: they say these denials never effect divorce because they are lies (the marriage actually exists), and a lie cannot create a legal effect – just like saying 'I never married you' does not invalidate an existing marriage. However, all three imams (Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn) agree that if the denial is accompanied by an oath, like 'By Allah, you are not my wife', then no divorce occurs even if intended. The reason is that the oath makes the statement a report (khabar) about the past, and since the husband is lying in his oath, it remains a lie but does not enact divorce. The commentary (hashiya) clarifies the exception of 'mudhākarat al-ṭalāq' and the distinction between court and religious reality.

Key Terms
مذاكرة الطلاق (mudhākarat al-ṭalāq): Context of discussing divorce; when the couple is actively talking about divorce, even general denial phrases are treated as divorce in court.
نفي الزوجية (nafy al-zawjiyyah): Denial of the marital relationship; phrases that state the marriage does not exist.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a husband says 'لست لي بامرأة' without intention to divorce, but in a context of discussing divorce, what is the ruling?
A) Divorce occurs both in court and religiously.
B) Divorce occurs in court but not religiously.
C) No divorce at all.
D) The marriage is annulled.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, denial of marriage can be a valid divorce only if intended or during divorce discussions (Abu Hanifa), but the Sahibayn reject it entirely; judicial validity may differ from religious reality.

Ruling on Kinayah without Intention and Exception in Muthakarat al-Talaq
States rule for kinayah without intention, with exception when discussing divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the default ruling for kinayah divorce and the exception during muthakarat al-talaq, and apply the distinction between qada' and diyanah.

Core Idea
A kinayah divorce requires intention—except when the couple is discussing divorce; then the court enforces it even without intention, but it is not a sin.

This lesson clarifies the Hanafi ruling on indirect expressions (kinayah) of divorce. The default rule is clear: without intention, no divorce occurs. However, there is a critical exception: when the spouses are engaged in a discussion of divorce (muthakarat al-talaq), the judge treats the expression as divorce, regardless of the husband's inner intention. This creates a distinction between the external ruling (qada') and the internal matter between the man and Allah (diyanah). Understanding this nuance prevents confusion in legal practice and personal accountability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
For any kinayah expression of divorce, the default is that divorce does not occur if the husband had no intention of divorce. Intention (niyyah) is essential.
2
Recognize the exception
If the spouses are in a context of discussing divorce (muthakarat al-talaq), the ruling changes. The commentary defines this as the wife demanding divorce for herself or for another wife.
3
Distinguish qada' and diyanah
During muthakarat al-talaq, divorce takes effect in the court (qada') even without intention. However, between the man and Allah (diyanah), it is not divorce unless he actually intended it. This means the judge enforces the divorce, but the husband is not sinful if he had no intention.
This is a key Hanafi principle: external ruling may differ from internal reality.
4
Apply the rule to scenarios
Given a specific scenario, determine whether divorce occurs in qada', diyanah, both, or neither, based on the presence of intention and the context of discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن لم تكن له نية الطلاق : لم يقع بهذه الألفاظ طلاق، إلا أن يكونا في مذاكرة الطلاق : فيقعُ بها الطلاق في القضاء، ولا يقع فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى، إلا أن ينوي به الطلاق .
* قوله: (فإن لم تكن له نيةٌ : لم يقع بهذه الألفاظ طلاق، إلا أن يكونا في مذاكرة الطلاق)، وهو أن تطالبه بالطلاق، أو تطالبه بطلاق غيرها. * قوله: (فيقعُ بها الطلاق في القضاء، ولا يقع فيما بينه وبين تعالى، إلا أن ينويه). الله
If he has no intention of divorce: divorce does not occur with these expressions, unless they are in a discussion of divorce: then divorce occurs in the judiciary, and does not occur between him and Allah Almighty, unless he intends divorce by it.
His statement: (If he has no intention: divorce does not occur with these expressions, unless they are in a discussion of divorce), and that is that she demands divorce from him, or she demands divorce from another woman. His statement: (then divorce occurs in the judiciary, and does not occur between him and the Almighty, unless he intends it).
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on kinayah (indirect expressions) of divorce. The default rule: if a husband uses a kinayah expression without intending divorce, no divorce takes place. However, there is an exception: if the spouses are engaged in a discussion of divorce (muthakarat al-talaq) — meaning the wife is asking for divorce either for herself or for another wife — then such expressions are considered divorce in the court (qada'), meaning the judge rules accordingly. But in the internal matter between the man and Allah, it is not divorce unless he actually intended it. The commentary clarifies the definition of 'muthakarat al-talaq' as the wife demanding divorce for herself or for another cowife.

Key Terms
كنايات الطلاق (kinayat al-talaq): Indirect expressions that may or may not imply divorce, requiring intention.
مذاكرة الطلاق (muthakarat al-talaq): A conversation where the wife asks for divorce for herself or another.

Classification of Kinayat and Their Rulings in Different Contexts
Definition and contextual rules of kinayat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of kinayat in Hanafi fiqh and apply the rules for when intention is required versus when it is not, based on the context (anger, discussion of divorce, or neutral).

Core Idea
Kinayat divorce: intention not needed in anger with non-insult words, but needed in calm; in muthakarat, words implying separation are court-effective.

Divorce can be effected not only by explicit words but also by indirect expressions (kinayat). However, the effectiveness of these expressions depends on the context and the presence of intention. In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi classification of kinayat into three types and the detailed rulings for each context: anger, discussion of divorce, and neutral situations.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the three categories
Identify the three types of kinayat mentioned: kinayat (e.g., 'You are forbidden'), madlulat (e.g., 'Observe your waiting period'), and tafwidat (e.g., 'Your matter is in your hand'). Note that they have different rules regarding intention.
Madlulat are understood as talaq in context; tafwidat delegate choice to the wife.
2
Determine the context
Classify the situation as one of three: muthakarat al-talaq (discussing divorce), anger/dispute, or calm/neutral. The ruling changes based on this.
If the couple is in a discussion of divorce, any word implying separation is treated as divorce outwardly.
3
Apply the relevant rule
In muthakarat, intention is not needed in court (but needed internally). In anger, non-insult words effect divorce without intention. In calm, intention is always required.
Words intended as insult in anger do not effect divorce unless intended.
4
Derive the final ruling
Based on the category and context, conclude whether a valid divorce has occurred. Consider whether the husband's claim of no intention is accepted.
In calm, his claim is accepted; in muthakarat, it is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال: لا حاجة لي فيك: ينوي الطلاق: فليس بطلاق. ولو قال: افلحي، أو فسختُ النكاح بيني وبينك: ينوي الطلاق كان طلاقاً.
وإن لم يكونا في مذاكرة الطلاق، وكانا في غضب، أو خصومةٍ : وَقَعَ الطلاق بكل لفظ لا يُقصد به السَّبُ، والشتيمة، ولم يقع بما يُقصد به السب والشتيمة، إلا أن ينويه.
أما إذا كانا في مذاكرة الطلاق فإنه يقع بكل لفظة تدل على الفرقة، كقوله : أنتِ حرام، وأمرك بيدك، واختاري، واعتدي، وأنت خليةٌ، وبريةٌ، وبائن؛ لأن هذه الألفاظ لما خرجت جواباً لسؤالها الطلاق كان ذلك طلاقاً في الظاهر.
وإنما لم يقع فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى؛ لأنه يحتمل أن يكون جواباً لها، ويحتمل أن يكون ابتداء، فلا يقع إلا بالنية.
قوله: (وإن لم يكونا في مذاكرة الطلاق، وكانا في غضب، أو خصومة: وقع الطلاق بكل لفظة لا يُقصد بها السب والشتيمة).
مثل: اعتدي، اختاري، أمرُكِ بيدكِ؛ لأن هذه الألفاظ لا تصلح للشتيمة، بل تحتمل الفُرقة، وحال الغضب حال الفرقة، فالظاهر من كلامه الفرقة.
* فحاصله أن الكنايات ثلاثة أقسام: كنايات، ومدلولات، وتفويضات. فالكنايات : أنتِ حرام، وبائن، وبيَّةٌ، وبَتْلة، وخلية، وبرية، واعتدي، واستبرئي رحمك.
فإن تكلم بها في مذاكرة الطلاق، وقال: لم أرد به الطلاق: لم يصدق.
And if he said: I have no need of you, intending divorce: it is not divorce. And if he said: Prosper, or I dissolved the marriage between me and you, intending divorce, it is divorce.
And if they are not in a discussion of divorce, and they are in anger or a dispute: divorce occurs with every word that is not intended as insult or abuse, and does not occur with what is intended as insult or abuse, unless he intends it.
As for when they are in a discussion of divorce, then it occurs with every word that indicates separation, such as his saying: You are forbidden (haram), your matter is in your hand, choose, observe your waiting period, you are free, you are separated, and you are irrevocably divorced; because these words, when uttered in response to her asking for divorce, are divorce outwardly.
And it does not occur between him and Allah Almighty because it could be a response to her or it could be an initiation, so it does not occur except with intention.
His statement: (And if they are not in a discussion of divorce, and they are in anger or a dispute: divorce occurs with every word that is not intended as insult or abuse).
Such as: Observe your waiting period, choose, your matter is in your hand; because these words are not fit for insult, but rather imply separation, and the state of anger is a state of separation, so the apparent meaning of his speech is separation.
* The summary is that the indirect expressions (kinayat) are three types: kinayat, madlulat, and tafwidat. The kinayat are: You are forbidden, irrevocably divorced, definitely divorced, separated, free, and you are waiting, and you are supposed to be in iddah.
If he speaks them in a discussion of divorce and says: I did not intend divorce by it, he is not believed.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage covers various rulings on kinayat expressions in different contexts. First, two examples: 'I have no need of you' with intention is not divorce, whereas 'Prosper' (iflahi) or 'I dissolved the marriage' with intention counts as divorce. Then the text discusses the context of anger: if they are not in a discussion of divorce but in anger or dispute, any word that is not normally used for insult (like 'choose', 'your matter is in your hand') is considered divorce even without explicit intention, because anger suggests a desire to end the marriage. However, if the word is usually an insult (like abusive language), it is not divorce unless intended. In the context of a discussion of divorce (muthakarat al-talaq), any word that implies separation (like 'you are forbidden', 'you are free') is treated as divorce in court, but internally requires intention. The commentary then summarizes the three categories: kinayat (indirect expressions like haram, baiin, etc.), madlulat (what is understood as talaq), and tafwidat (delegation of divorce). When spoken in muthakarat al-talaq, the speaker's claim of not intending divorce is not accepted in court.

Key Terms
كنايات (kinayat): Indirect expressions that can imply divorce but require intention or context to be effective.
مدلولات (madlulat): Expressions that are understood to mean divorce in the context; they effect divorce without intention in anger or muthakarat.
تفويضات (tafwidat): Expressions where the husband delegates the choice of divorce to the wife, like 'Your matter is in your hand'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of kinayat into three types and the contextual rules (anger, muthakarat, neutral) determine whether intention is required for a valid divorce.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
12 min
Rulings on Kināyāt in Talaq Across Contexts
Commentary on al-Quduri's matn regarding kināyāt in divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify divorce allusions (kināyāt) into three categories and state the Hanafi ruling for each based on the context of the pronouncement.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, whether an allusive word counts as divorce depends on intention and context: anger, discussion, or satisfaction.

Divorce is not always explicit; words like 'you are forbidden' or 'go' can imply it. The Hanafi school classifies these allusive words (kināyāt) into three categories—kināyāt proper, madlūlāt, and tafwīḍāt—and applies different rulings depending on whether the couple is in a divorce discussion, angry, or content. This passage from Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah explains these distinctions, enabling precise application of the law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
List the three types: kināyāt (eight specific words like 'you are forbidden'), madlūlāt (words like 'go', 'stand'), and tafwīḍāt (delegations like 'your affair is in your hand'). Note which words belong to each category.
The eight kināyāt are: حرام, بائن, بَيَّة, بَتْلَة, خلية, برية, اعتدي, استبرئي رحمك.
2
Understand the contextual factors
Recognize three contexts: mudhākarat al-ṭalāq (discussion of divorce initiated by the wife), riḍā (satisfaction/calm), and ghaḍab (anger/dispute). Each context affects whether the husband's intention is presumed or he is believed.
Context is key: in mudhākara, the appearance is divorce; in riḍā, intention is required; in ghaḍab, some words are treated as insults.
3
Apply the ruling for kināyāt proper
For the eight kināyāt words, determine: in mudhākara, the husband is not believed if he denies intention; in riḍā, he is believed; in ghaḍab, he is believed for five specific words (حرام, بائن, بية, خلية, برية) because they can be insults, but for the remaining three (بتلة, اعتدي, استبرئي), divorce occurs and he is not believed.
The five words in anger are believed because they are suitable for insult; the others are not suitable for insult, so divorce stands.
4
Apply the ruling for madlūlāt
Madlūlāt (indicative words) only become divorce if the husband intends it. If intended, it is a bā'in (irrevocable) divorce; if he intends three, three occur. If no intention, no divorce, regardless of context.
Examples: اذهبي, قومي, استري, etc.
5
Apply the ruling for tafwīḍāt
Tafwīḍāt (delegations): In anger, if the wife responds by choosing divorce, the husband is not believed if he denies divorce. The type of divorce depends on her wording: 'I choose myself' (اخترت نفسي) gives a bā'in divorce; 'I divorce myself' (طلقت نفسي) gives one raj'ī divorce.
This applies only when the wife responds; the husband's original delegation is the trigger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق
وإن لم يكونا في مذاكرة الطلاق، وكانا في غضب، أو خصومةٍ : وَقَعَ الطلاق بكل لفظ لا يُقصد به السَّبُ، والشتيمة، ولم يقع بما يُقصد به السب والشتيمة، إلا أن ينويه.
أما إذا كانا في مذاكرة الطلاق فإنه يقع بكل لفظة تدل على الفرقة، كقوله : أنتِ حرام، وأمرك بيدك، واختاري، واعتدي، وأنت خليةٌ، وبريةٌ، وبائن؛ لأن هذه الألفاظ لما خرجت جواباً لسؤالها الطلاق كان ذلك طلاقاً في الظاهر.
وإنما لم يقع فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى؛ لأنه يحتمل أن يكون جواباً لها، ويحتمل أن يكون ابتداء، فلا يقع إلا بالنية.
قوله: (وإن لم يكونا في مذاكرة الطلاق، وكانا في غضب، أو خصومة: وقع الطلاق بكل لفظة لا يُقصد بها السب والشتيمة).
مثل: اعتدي، اختاري، أمرُكِ بيدكِ؛ لأن هذه الألفاظ لا تصلح للشتيمة، بل تحتمل الفُرقة، وحال الغضب حال الفرقة، فالظاهر من كلامه الفرقة.
* فحاصله أن الكنايات ثلاثة أقسام: كنايات، ومدلولات، وتفويضات. فالكنايات : أنتِ حرام، وبائن، وبيَّةٌ، وبَتْلة، وخلية، وبرية، واعتدي، واستبرئي رحمك.
فإن تكلم بها في مذاكرة الطلاق، وقال: لم أرد به الطلاق: لم يصدق.
وإن تكلم بها في حالة الرضا : إن نوى بها الطلاق: وقع، وإلا: فلا، ويُصدق أنه لم ينو الطلاق.
وإن تكلَّم بها في حالة الغصب : صُدق في خمسة ألفاظ أنه لم يُرِد بها الطلاق، وهي أنت حرام ،وبائن، وبيَّةٌ، وخلية، وبرية؛ لأن هذه تصلح للشتيمة، تحتمل بائن من الدين، وبتةٌ من المروءة، وخليةٌ من الخير، وبريةٌ من الإسلام، وحرام الاجتماع معك، والحال حال الشتيمة، فالظاهر أنه أرادها ولم يُرد الطلاق.
والمدلولات : اذهبي وقومي واستري، وتقنعي، واخرجي، والحقي بأهلك، وحَبْلُكِ على غاربك، ولا نكاح بيني وبينك، وأشباه ذلك. فإن نوى بها الطلاق وقع بائناً، وإن نوى ثلاثاً: فثلاث، وإن لم ينو : لا يكون طلاقاً، سواء كانا في حالة الرضا أو الغضب أو مذاكرة الطلاق.
والتفويضات : أمرك بيدك، اختاري.
ففي حالة الغضب: لا يُصدق في التفويضات، ولا في الكنايات الرجعية، يعني لا يُصدق في التفويضات إذا قالت مجيبةً له: اخترتُ نفسي، أو: طلقت نفسي.
ثم في قولها اخترتُ نفسي يقع طلقة بائنة، وفي قولها: طلقت نفسي: واحدة رجعية.
(۱) وفي نسخ: واستبرئي.
Book of Divorce. If they are not in a discussion of divorce, and they are in anger or dispute: divorce occurs with every word that is not intended as insult or abuse, and it does not occur with what is intended as insult or abuse, unless he intends it. However, if they are in a discussion of divorce, then it occurs with every word indicating separation, like his saying: 'You are forbidden', 'your affair is in your hand', 'choose', 'observe your waiting period', 'you are free', 'you are disassociated', and 'you are separated'; because these words, when uttered as a response to her request for divorce, are divorce in appearance. It does not occur between him and Allah Almighty because it could be a response to her or an initiation, so it only occurs with intention. His saying: (If they are not in a discussion of divorce, and they are in anger or dispute: divorce occurs with every word that is not intended as insult or abuse) like: 'observe your waiting period', 'choose', 'your affair is in your hand'; because these words are not suitable for insult, but rather imply separation, and the state of anger is a state of separation, so the apparent meaning of his speech is separation. The summary is that kināyāt are three types: kināyāt, madlūlāt, and tafwīḍāt. The kināyāt are: 'you are forbidden', 'separated', 'cut off', 'severed', 'free', 'disassociated', 'observe your waiting period', and 'ascertain the purity of your womb'. If he speaks them in a discussion of divorce and says: 'I did not intend divorce', he is not believed. If he speaks them in a state of satisfaction: if he intends divorce by them, it occurs; otherwise, no, and he is believed that he did not intend divorce. If he speaks them in a state of anger: he is believed in five words that he did not intend divorce by them: 'you are forbidden', 'separated', 'cut off', 'free', and 'disassociated'; because these are suitable for insult, implying 'separated from religion', 'cut off from manliness', 'free from good', 'disassociated from Islam', and 'forbidden to associate with you', and the situation is one of insult, so the apparent meaning is that he intended them as insult, not divorce. The madlūlāt: 'go', 'stand', 'cover', 'veil yourself', 'leave', 'join your family', 'your rope is on your shoulder', 'there is no marriage between me and you', and the like. If he intends divorce by them, it occurs as a bā'in (irrevocable) divorce; if he intends three, then three; if he does not intend, it is not divorce, whether they are in a state of satisfaction, anger, or discussion of divorce. The tafwīḍāt: 'your affair is in your hand', 'choose'. In a state of anger, he is not believed regarding the tafwīḍāt, nor regarding the raj'ī (revocable) kināyāt, meaning he is not believed in the tafwīḍāt if she responds to him: 'I choose myself' or 'I divorce myself.' Then in her saying 'I choose myself', a bā'in divorce occurs; in her saying 'I divorce myself', one raj'ī divorce occurs. (1) In some copies: 'and ascertain the purity of your womb'.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage from Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah, a commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri, explains the ruling of divorce pronouncements using kināyāt (allusive words). The commentator first addresses the case when the couple is not already discussing divorce but are in a state of anger or dispute. In such a state, divorce occurs with any word that is not intended as an insult or abuse; but if the word is typically used as an insult, divorce does not occur unless the husband actually intended it. Then the commentator discusses when they are in a discussion of divorce (mudhākarat al-ṭalāq): if the husband responds to his wife's request for divorce with any word indicating separation, it is considered divorce in appearance, but between him and Allah it only counts if he intended it. The commentator then introduces a three-part classification of kināyāt: (1) Kināyāt proper: words like 'you are forbidden', 'you are separated', etc. (2) Madlūlāt: words that indicate an action like 'go', 'stand', etc. (3) Tafwīḍāt: delegations like 'your affair is in your hand' or 'choose'. For the kināyāt proper, the ruling depends on context: in a divorce discussion, the husband is not believed if he claims no intention; in satisfaction, he is believed; in anger, he is believed for five specific words (حرام، بائن، بية، خلية، برية) because they can be insults. For the madlūlāt, they only become divorce if intended, regardless of context, and if intended, it is a bā'in (irrevocable) divorce. For the tafwīḍāt, in anger, the husband is not believed if he denies divorce after the wife chooses divorce; and when the wife says 'I choose myself' it is a bā'in divorce, while 'I divorce myself' is a raj'ī (revocable) divorce. This passage demonstrates the Hanafi approach of considering the external context and the husband's intention.

Key Terms
كنايات (kināyāt): Allusive words in divorce that do not explicitly say 'divorce' but imply it, and require intention for effect.
مدلولات (madlūlāt): Indicative words like 'go', 'stand', etc., that have a fixed meaning of divorce only if intended.
تفويضات (tafwīḍāt): Delegations where the husband gives the wife the power to choose divorce, like 'your affair is in your hand'.
مذاكرة الطلاق (mudhākarat al-ṭalāq): A conversation where the couple is discussing divorce, often initiated by the wife's request.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) أنت حرام (you are forbidden)
C) اذهبي (go)
D) اعتدي (observe waiting period)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the effect of allusive divorce words depends on their category and the context of the pronouncement, requiring careful analysis of both linguistic meaning and external circumstances.

Divorce in Anger and Kinayah: Intention and Classification
Rulings on explicit and implied divorce words with anger/coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between explicit (ṣarīḥ) and implied (kināyah) divorce words and state the Hanafi ruling on each based on the husband's state (anger vs satisfaction).

Core Idea
In anger, five specific words are presumed not divorce; only implied words require intent.

Scholars of the Hanafi school carefully consider the husband's emotional state and choice of words when pronouncing divorce. This lesson explores the distinction between explicit and implied words, and how intention affects validity. Understanding these rulings prevents unjust divorces and protects marital bonds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two emotional states
The passage distinguishes between satisfaction (riḍā) and anger/coercion (ghaḍab). In satisfaction, intention determines the ruling; in anger, special rules apply to certain words.
2
List the five words believed in anger
In anger, the husband is believed regarding five words: ḥarām, bā'in, battah, khaliyyah, bariyyah. He can claim he intended insult, not divorce.
These words have dual meanings—insult or divorce—so the default in anger is non-divorce.
3
Understand kināyah (implied) words
Implied words like 'Go', 'Leave', etc., require intent (niyyah) to effect divorce. Intent can be for one or three, leading to bā'in divorce accordingly.
Kināyah words do not trigger divorce automatically; the husband's inner intent is crucial.
4
Examine delegations (tafwīḍāt)
When the husband delegates divorce to the wife ('Your affair is in your hand'), in anger he is not believed regarding the delegation. If the wife responds, her pronouncement effects divorce: 'I chose myself' gives bā'in, 'I divorced myself' gives raj'ī.
This prevents the husband from revoking the delegation under pressure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if he speaks them in a state of satisfaction: if he intended divorce, it occurs; otherwise, no divorce occurs, and he is believed that he did not intend divorce. And if he speaks them in a state of coercion: he is believed regarding five words that he did not intend divorce: 'you are forbidden', 'you are bā'in', 'you are battah', 'you are khaliyyah', and 'you is bariyyah'; because these are suitable for insult, they can mean: separated from religion, cut off from manliness, empty of goodness, free from Islam, and forbidden to associate with you, and the situation is that of insult, so it is apparent that he intended the insult and did not intend divorce. And the implied words: 'Go', 'Get up', 'Cover', 'Veil', 'Leave', 'Join your family', 'Your rope is on your hump', 'There is no marriage between me and you', and similar. If he intended divorce, it occurs as bā'in; if he intended three, it is three; and if he did not intend, it is not divorce, whether in a state of satisfaction, anger, or discussing divorce. Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayrah explains the rulings on divorce pronouncements depending on the state of the husband. It distinguishes between situations of satisfaction (riḍā) and coercion/anger (ghaḍab). In a state of satisfaction, the validity of a divorce pronouncement depends on intention: if he intended divorce, it occurs; otherwise, he is believed that he did not intend it. In a state of coercion or anger, he is believed regarding five specific words (ḥarām, bā'in, battah, khaliyyah, bariyyah) that he did not intend divorce, because these words are commonly used for insult and can be interpreted in non-divorce meanings. The passage then lists 'implied words' (kināyāt) such as 'Go', 'Leave', etc. For these, if he intended divorce, a bā'in divorce occurs; if he intended three, three occur; if no intention, no divorce. This applies regardless of the state. Finally, it discusses delegations (tafwīḍāt) such as 'Your affair is in your hand' and 'Choose'. In anger, the husband is not believed regarding delegations and revocable implied words. When the wife responds with 'I chose myself', a bā'in divorce occurs; if she says 'I divorced myself', one revocable divorce occurs.

Key Terms
بائن (bā'in): An irrevocable divorce that severs the marital bond immediately, requiring a new marriage contract for remarriage.
كنايات (kināyāt): Implied words that require intention to effect divorce.
تفويضات (tafwīḍāt): Delegation of divorce power to the wife, e.g., 'Your affair is in your hand'.
رجعية (raj'iyyah): A revocable divorce that allows the husband to take back his wife during iddah without a new contract.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rulings on divorce protect against hasty breakups by considering the husband's emotional state and the type of words used—intent matter for implied words, and five specific words in anger are presumed not to be divorce.

Describing Divorce with Intensifying Words
Ruling that descriptive words intensify divorce and make it bā'in

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the revocable and irrevocable effects of intensifying descriptions in divorce and state the mufta bihi position in the Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad dispute.

Core Idea
When a divorce is described with words of intensity, it becomes irrevocable (bā'in) — unless three divorces are intended.

This passage explores a crucial nuance: when a husband pronounces divorce but adds intensifying descriptions like 'severest divorce' or 'like a mountain', the divorce becomes bā'in (irrevocable). However, an intra-Hanafi disagreement arises when the husband intends only one divorce but uses such language. We will examine the dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and see how al-Hidayah resolves it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
The act is pronouncing divorce with descriptive intensifiers. The ruling is that it makes the divorce bā'in.
This is the general rule, covering phrases like 'bā'in', 'severest divorce', etc.
2
Examine the examples
The text lists phrases such as 'bā'in', 'ashadd al-ṭalāq', 'fāḥish al-ṭalāq', 'ṭalāq al-shayṭān', 'ka-al-jabal', 'mal' al-bayt'. Each triggers the bā'in effect.
These are not exhaustive; any word of increase or intensity qualifies.
3
Analyse the intention scenario
When the husband says 'You are divorced with the ugliest divorce' and intends one divorce, there is dispute: Abu Yusuf says revocable (rajʿī), Muhammad says bā'in.
The key difference is whether the description inherently adds irrevocability.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
According to al-Hidayah, the preferred view is that it is bā'in unless three are intended. If three intended, it's three. If no intention, it is bā'in.
This is based on mentioning the verbal noun (maṣdar) which can indicate count if intended.
5
Apply the rule to other examples
In Sharhu al-Hidayah, 'like a thousand' with no intention is bā'in by both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, showing the rule is settled.
The later practice aligns with Muhammad's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if he describes divorce with a type of increase and intensity, it becomes bā'in, such as saying: 'You are divorced bā'in', or 'divorced with the severest divorce', or 'the foulest divorce', or 'devil's divorce', or 'innovative divorce', or 'like a mountain', or 'filling the house'. * His statement: (And if he describes divorce with a type of increase and intensity, it becomes bā'in); because divorce occurs merely by the word, so if he describes it with an increase, it conveys a meaning not in its word. Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of describing the divorce with words of intensity. The ruling is that such descriptions make the divorce bā'in (irrevocable). The reason given is that divorce occurs merely by the word, so adding an intensifying description adds an extra meaning (irrevocability). The examples include 'severest divorce', 'foulest divorce', 'like a mountain', etc. Then an intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: if one says 'You are divorced with the ugliest divorce' and intended one divorce, Abu Yusuf holds it is revocable (raj'ī) while Muhammad holds it is bā'in. The relied-upon position according to al-Hidayah is that it is one bā'in unless he intended three, in which case it is three, due to mentioning the source (i.e., the verbal noun). According to the commentary 'Sharhu al-Hidayah', if one says 'like a thousand', if he intended three it is three, if he intended one it is bā'in, and if no intention it is bā'in by both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).

Key Terms
بائن (bā'in): Irrevocable divorce that cannot be reversed during iddah.
زيادة وشدة (ziyādah wa shiddah): Words of increase and intensity that describe the divorce, making it bā'in.

Ruling on 'One Like a Thousand' Divorce
Ruling on the phrase 'واحدة كألف' and its effect as one ba'in divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce by the phrase 'one like a thousand' and explain why it cannot be three even if intended.

Core Idea
The word 'one' in 'one like a thousand' limits the divorce to one — number cannot be overridden by simile.

This passage addresses a specific phrase in divorce: 'واحدة كألف' (one like a thousand). Though Imam Muhammad initially considered it could be three divorces, the settled Hanafi position is that it is only one ba'in divorce by consensus. Understanding why this phrase is limited to one involves analyzing the literal word 'one' and its capacity.

Guided Path
1
State the consensus ruling
The text concludes that saying 'one like a thousand' results in one ba'in divorce by consensus of the Hanafi imams.
ركز على كلمة 'إجماعاً' في النص.
2
Note the initial disagreement
Imam Muhammad initially held it could be three if intended, because the phrase involves a number and similitude in number, similar to 'like the number of a thousand'.
فهم لماذا اعتبر محمد رواية الثلاث.
3
Understand the reason for the final ruling
The word 'واحدة' (one) cannot accommodate three. The similitude does not override the specified number; it only emphasizes multiplicity, not the count itself.
العلة: الواحدة لا تحتمل الثلاث.
4
Distinguish between internal intention and judicial validity
If the husband intended one ba'in, his intention is accepted before Allah but not in court, because the apparent meaning is clear and conflicts with his claim.
هذا يبين الفرق بين الباطن والظاهر في القضاء.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال محمد: هي ثلاث؛ لأنه عدد، فيُراد به التشبيه في العدد، كما إذا قال : كعدد الألف. قال محمد: فإن نوى واحدة بائنة: دينته فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى، ولا أُدينه في القضاء. وإن قال: واحدة كألف: فهي واحدة بائنة، إجماعاً. ولا تكون ثلاثاً وإن نوى؛ لأن الواحدة لا تحتمل الثلاث.
Muhammad said: It is three because it is a number, so the intent is similitude in number, just as if he said: Like the number of a thousand. Muhammad said: If he intended one ba'in (irrevocable divorce), I would accept his intention between him and Allah, but I would not accept it in the judicial authority. And if he says: one like a thousand, then it is one ba'in divorce by consensus. And it does not become three even if he intended it, because one cannot accommodate three.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the phrase 'one like a thousand' in divorce. Muhammad initially thought it might be three due to the number comparison, but later clarifies that the final ruling, by consensus of the Hanafi school, is that it counts as one irrevocable (ba'in) divorce. The reason is that the word 'one' specifies the number, and the similitude ('like a thousand') does not override that. If the husband intended three, it is not valid because 'one' cannot be interpreted as three. However, if he intended one ba'in, his intention is accepted before Allah but not in court due to the apparent meaning.

Key Terms
بائنة (bāʾinah): An irrevocable divorce that does not allow remarriage without a new contract (ba'in sughra).
إجماعاً (ijmāʿan): By consensus of the Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar).

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Divorce by Simile: Ba'in vs Raj'i
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the effect of divorce similes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar on whether a divorce simile yields ba'in or raj'i, and apply the correct ruling to specific expressions.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, the effect of a divorce simile depends on whether the expression implies greatness—explicitly, inherently, or not at all.

Not all divorce similes produce the same result. The Hanafi school has a detailed internal disagreement on whether comparing divorce to an object like a mountain or a pinhead makes the divorce revocable (raj'i) or irrevocable (ba'in). This passage from Al-Khujandi's commentary on Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar lays out the criteria used by the early Hanafi imams. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly classifying divorce and advising on its legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of divorce similes
First, note that the passage treats two types: numerical similes (e.g., 'like the number of a thousand') and object similes (e.g., 'like a mountain'). The former leads to three divorces by consensus; the latter triggers the intra-Hanafi dispute.
Numerical similes are unambiguous in indicating multiplicity.
2
Extract each imam's general principle for object similes
Read the passage carefully to find the principle for each imam: Abu Hanifa says any simile yields ba'in; Abu Yusuf makes it conditional on the presence of the word 'greatness'; Zufar looks at the inherent quality of the object. Muhammad's position is reported as uncertain.
The phrase 'متى شبه الطلاق بشيء' indicates Abu Hanifa's general rule.
3
Work through the clarifying examples
Apply the principles to the specific examples given: 'like the greatness of a pinhead', 'like a pinhead', 'like a mountain', 'like the greatness of a mountain'. Determine the outcome according to each imam.
Note where consensus occurs, e.g., with 'greatness of a mountain' all agree on ba'in.
4
Handle numerical similes with non-numerical objects
Examine the final portion of the passage about expressions like 'number of the sun' or 'number of dust'. These treat the comparison as one divorce (ba'in or raj'i depending on the imam) unless the husband intends a specific number.
Muhammad's position on 'like stars' introduces a linguistic interpretation (light vs. number).
5
Note the authority of the rulings
The text mentions 'في ظاهر الرواية' for the first ruling (ba'in with consensus) and 'إجماع' for specific cases. Recognize that the well-known narration (zahir al-riwayah) forms the authoritative basis of the school, though the ikhtilaf remains valid.
The mufta bihi may vary by context; later scholars often preferred Abu Hanifa's or Abu Yusuf's view.

- وإن قال : أنت طالق كعدد الألف، أو مثل عدد الألف، أو كعدد ثلاث، أو مثل عدد ثلاث : فهي ثلاث وإن نوى غير ذلك. - قال الخُجندي: إذا قال: أنتِ طالق مثل الجبل، أو مثل عِظَم الجبل، أو ملء الكون، أو ملء البيت، أو كألف، أو مثل ألف: كان بائناً، في ظاهر الرواية، بالإجماع. والأصل: أن عند أبي حنيفة متى شبه الطلاق بشيء: يقع بائناً بأي شيء شبهه، صغيراً كان أو كبيراً، سواء ذَكَرَ العِظَم أو لا. وعند أبي يوسف: إن ذكر العِظَم كان بائناً، وإلا: فلا، سواء كان المشبه به صغيراً أو كبيراً، وإن لم يذكر العِظَم: يكون رجعياً. وعند زفر: إن كان المشبه به يوصف بالشدة والعظم: كان بائناً، وإلا : فهو رجعي. ومحمد قيل : مع أبي حنيفة، وقيل: مع أبي يوسف. بيانه: إذا قال: أنت طالق مثل عِظَم رأس الإبرة: كان بائناً عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وقال زفر: هو رجعي. وإن قال: مثل رأس الإبرة، أو مثل حبَّة الخردل: فهو بائن عند أبي حنيفة، ورجعي عند أبي يوسف وزفر. وإن قال: مثل الجبل: كان بائناً عند أبي حنيفة وزفر، وقال أبو يوسف: رجعي. وإن قال : مثل عظم الجبل: كان بائناً، إجماعاً. - فإن نوى بهذه الألفاظ كلها ثلاثاً: كان ثلاثاً، بالإجماع. وإن قال : أنت طالق مثل عدد كذا، وأضاف إلى شيء ليس له عدد، كما إذا قال : أنت طالق عدد الشمس، أو عدد القمر: فهي واحدة بائنة عند أبي حنيفة، ورجعية عند أبي يوسف. ولو قال: كالنجوم: فواحدة عن عند محمد؛ لأن معناه كالنجوم ضياء، إلا أن ينوي العدد: فيكون ثلاثاً. وإن قال: أنتِ طالق عدد التراب فهي واحدة عند أبي يوسف، وثلاث عند محمد. وإن قال: عدد الرمل : فهي ثلاث، إجماعاً.
And if he says: You are divorced like the number of a thousand, or like the number of a thousand, or like the number of three, or like the number of three: then it is three even if he intended otherwise. Al-Khujandi said: If he says: You are divorced like a mountain, or like the greatness of a mountain, or the fill of the universe, or the fill of the house, or like a thousand, or like a thousand: it is ba'in, in the apparent narration, by consensus. And the principle: According to Abu Hanifa, whenever divorce is likened to something, it occurs as ba'in regardless of what it is likened to, small or large, whether he mentioned greatness or not. According to Abu Yusuf: If he mentioned greatness, it is ba'in; otherwise, no, whether the object of simile is small or large, and if he did not mention greatness: it is raj'i. According to Zufar: If the object of simile is described with severity and greatness, it is ba'in; otherwise, it is raj'i. And regarding Muhammad, it is said he is with Abu Hanifa, and it is said he is with Abu Yusuf. Its clarification: If he says: You are divorced like the greatness of a pinhead: it is ba'in according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Zufar said: It is raj'i. If he says: like a pinhead, or like a mustard seed: then it is ba'in according to Abu Hanifa, and raj'i according to Abu Yusuf and Zufar. If he says: like a mountain: it is ba'in according to Abu Hanifa and Zufar, and Abu Yusuf said: raj'i. If he says: like the greatness of a mountain: it is ba'in by consensus. If he intends three by all these expressions, it becomes three by consensus. If he says: You are divorced like the number of such-and-such, and adds it to something that has no number, like if he says: You are divorced by the number of the sun, or the number of the moon: then it is one ba'in according to Abu Hanifa, and raj'i according to Abu Yusuf. If he says: like the stars: then it is one according to Muhammad, because its meaning is 'like the stars in light', unless he intends number, then it is three. If he says: You are divorced by the number of dust: then it is one according to Abu Yusuf, and three according to Muhammad. If he says: the number of sand: then it is three by consensus.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of comparing divorce to numbers or objects. It first rules that when likened to a specific number like 'عدد الألف' (number of a thousand) or 'عدد ثلاث' (number of three), the divorce is three regardless of intention, because the phrase indicates multiplicity. Then it discusses general comparisons without numbers (like 'like a mountain') where the principle determines whether the divorce is ba'in (irrevocable) or raj'i (revocable). The three Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Zufar) differ on the criterion: Abu Hanifa says any simile makes it ba'in; Abu Yusuf says only if greatness is mentioned explicitly; Zufar says only if the object is inherently described as great/serious. Muhammad's position is ambiguous. The clarification (بيانه) provides examples: a pinhead with 'greatness' is ba'in per Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf but raj'i per Zufar; a pinhead without 'greatness' is ba'in only per Abu Hanifa; a mountain without 'greatness' is ba'in per Abu Hanifa and Zufar but raj'i per Abu Yusuf; a mountain with 'greatness' is ba'in by consensus. Then the passage notes that if the husband intends three by these expressions, it becomes three (by consensus). Finally, it addresses numbers added to things without numbers (like sun, moon): result is one ba'in for Abu Hanifa, one raj'i for Abu Yusuf. For 'like stars', Muhammad's position is that it refers to light unless number intended. For 'number of dust', Abu Yusuf says one, Muhammad says three; for 'number of sand', three by consensus.

Key Terms
بائن (bāʾin): An irrevocable divorce that does not allow the husband to take back his wife without a new marriage contract during the waiting period.
رجعي (rajʿī): A revocable divorce where the husband can take back his wife during the waiting period without a new contract.
ظاهر الرواية (ẓāhir al-riwāyah): The apparent/well-known narration from Imam Muhammad's six books (the basis of the school).
تشبيه (tashbīh): A simile or comparison used in the divorce pronouncement (e.g., 'like a mountain').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following statements is TRUE regarding the Hanafi ikhtilaf on divorce similes?
A) Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf agree that any simile without greatness is raj'i.
B) Zufar considers 'like a pinhead' to be ba'in because it is small.
C) For 'like the greatness of a mountain', all imams agree it is ba'in (consensus).
D) Muhammad's position is that 'like a thousand' is one divorce.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the effect of divorce similes is not uniform; the outcome depends on whether the expression implies greatness, and different imams apply different criteria.

Divorce by Simile: Ba'in or Raj'i? Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on divorce by simile with ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on divorce by simile and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Zufar, identifying the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A divorce simile like 'thickness of a needle head' yields ba'in according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; Zufar holds it is raj'i.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the legal effect of a divorce pronounced by a comparative simile. Though the wording does not state the number, the simile's emphasis on smallness leads Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf to classify it as ba'in (irrevocable). Zufar maintains it remains raj'i (revocable) because the simile does not alter the default. The school's relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pronouncement
The husband says 'You are divorced like the thickness of a needle head' – a simile comparing the divorce to a very small object.
2
Note the imams and their positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule it is ba'in; Zufar says it is raj'i. Recognize that this is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
Zufar is a Hanafi imam, so the dispute is within the school.
3
Understand the legal categories
Ba'in means the husband cannot take his wife back during iddah without a new marriage; raj'i allows return without a new contract.
The difference lies in revocability.
4
Analyze the reasoning
The simile emphasizes smallness, implying finality; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf see this as indicating a complete irrevocable divorce. Zufar views the simile as merely descriptive, not affecting the default raj'i status.
The interpretation hinges on whether the simile alters the legal nature.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The Hanafi school follows Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Thus, the fatwa is that such a divorce is ba'in.
Zufar's view is recognized but not operative for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا قال: أنت طالق مثل عِظَم رأس الإبرة: كان بائناً عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وقال زفر: هو رجعي.
If he said: 'You are divorced like the thickness of a needle head' – it is a ba'in divorce according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Zufar said: It is a raj'i divorce.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal effect of a divorce pronounced with a comparative simile. When a husband says 'like the thickness of a needle head', Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider the divorce to be ba'in (irrevocable) because the comparison indicates finality and the numeral 'one' is not expressed. Zufar holds it to be raj'i (revocable) because the word 'like' does not negate the possibility of counting it as one. The difference stems from whether the simile is seen as emphasizing the smallness (which aligns with ba'in) or as merely descriptive (allowing raj'i). The Hanafi school follows the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf as the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
بائن (bāʾin): An irrevocable divorce where the husband cannot take back his wife during the iddah without a new marriage contract; the wife becomes completely separated.
رجعي (rajʿī): A revocable divorce where the husband may take his wife back during the iddah without a new marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is a ba'in divorce
C) It is three divorces
D) It is not a valid divorce

Key Takeaway
A divorce pronounced by a simile comparing to a small object is considered ba'in (irrevocable) in the Hanafi school, following Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Divorce by Simile to a Needle Head or Mustard Seed
Ruling on divorce simile with ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on divorce by simile to a needle head or mustard seed and explain the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A divorce simile using 'like' can be ba'in or raj'i depending on how finality is interpreted.

This lesson examines a specific case of talaq by simile (tashbih) where the husband says 'like a needle head' or 'like a mustard seed'. The Hanafi school has an internal dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa deems this divorce ba'in (irrevocable), while the two disciples (Sahibayn) consider it raj'i (revocable). Understanding the reasoning behind each view sharpens your grasp of the rules governing the language of divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wording
The husband says 'You are divorced like a needle head' or 'like a mustard seed.' Note the word 'like' (mithl) which creates a simile.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
Abu Hanifa rules this as ba'in (irrevocable) divorce. He views the simile as implying finality, similar to saying 'like the thickness of a rope' where the divorce is also ba'in.
The absence of the word 'thickness' does not matter; the mere simile conveys finality.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling
Abu Yusuf and Zufar rule it as raj'i (revocable). They argue the simile is purely descriptive and does not alter the revocable nature of a single divorce.
For them, 'like' compares the divorce itself, not a dimension implying finality.
4
Note the practical consequence
If ba'in, the husband cannot remarry her without a new marriage contract. If raj'i, he can take her back during iddah without renewal.
This ikhtilaf directly affects the couple's ability to reconcile.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's: the divorce is ba'in.
This is the view given preference by later Hanafi jurists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قال: مثل رأس الإبرة، أو مثل حبَّة الخردل: فهو بائن عند أبي حنيفة، ورجعي عند أبي يوسف وزفر.
And if he said: 'Like a needle head' or 'like a mustard seed' – it is ba'in according to Abu Hanifa, and raj'i according to Abu Yusuf and Zufar.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
Here, the simile uses 'like a needle head' or 'like a mustard seed' without the word 'thickness'. Abu Hanifa rules it as ba'in, while Abu Yusuf and Zufar consider it raj'i. The difference may lie in the absence of the word 'thickness', which for Abu Hanifa still conveys finality, but for the other two it is a mere description that does not alter the revocable nature.

Key Terms
مثل (mithl): A word of comparison; in divorce, it creates a simile that may affect the legal classification of the divorce.
بائن (ba'in): Irrevocable divorce; the husband cannot take back his wife during iddah without a new marriage contract.
رجعي (raj'i): Revocable divorce; the husband can take back his wife during the iddah without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is raj'i
C) It is three divorces
D) It is invalid

Key Takeaway
A divorce expressed as a simile to a needle head or mustard seed is ba'in according to Abu Hanifa, but raj'i according to Abu Yusuf and Zufar; the relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's.

Divorce by Comparing to Mountain: Ba'in for Abu Hanifa and Zufar, Raj'i for Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi ruling on divorce by simile to a mountain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Zufar, and Abu Yusuf on divorce by simile to a mountain, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf between ba'in and raj'i rulings.

Core Idea
When a husband compares his wife to a mountain, Abu Hanifa and Zufar rule it ba'in, while Abu Yusuf rules raj'i—the practical consequence is remarriage without a new contract.

The lesson examines a specific case of divorce by simile (tashbih) where the object of comparison is a mountain. Even within the Hanafi school, there is ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa and Zufar consider it ba'in (irrevocable), while Abu Yusuf considers it raj'i (revocable). This dispute has direct implications for the couple's ability to reconcile.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions
Determine the ruling of each Hanafi imam on the divorce simile 'like a mountain'. Abu Hanifa and Zufar say ba'in; Abu Yusuf says raj'i.
Note that Zufar sides with Abu Hanifa here, unlike some other cases.
2
Understand the nature of the dispute
The dispute is about whether the simile makes the divorce ba'in (final) or raj'i (revocable).
The essence: does the simile indicate finality or retain revocability?
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if ba'in, the husband cannot take her back without a new marriage contract and her consent; if raj'i, he can revoke within the iddah.
This directly affects the couple's options for reconciliation.
4
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon view in the school is Abu Hanifa's (ba'in).
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قال: مثل الجبل: كان بائناً عند أبي حنيفة وزفر، وقال أبو يوسف: رجعي.
And if he said: 'Like a mountain' – it is ba'in according to Abu Hanifa and Zufar, and Abu Yusuf said: raj'i.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
Here, the simile is to a large object (mountain). Abu Hanifa and Zufar rule it ba'in, while Abu Yusuf dissents, ruling raj'i. This is the reverse of the earlier pattern where Zufar was with Abu Yusuf. The size of the object does not change the underlying principle: Abu Hanifa and Zufar view the simile as making the divorce ba'in, while Abu Yusuf sees it as raj'i. The school's relied-upon view is that of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
بائن (ba'in): Irrevocable divorce that severs the marriage immediately; the husband cannot take back his wife without a new marriage contract.
رجعي (raj'i): Revocable divorce where the husband can take back his wife during the iddah without a new contract.
طلاق تشبيه (talaq al-tashbih): Divorce pronounced by comparing the wife to something, e.g., 'like a mountain'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Ba'in
B) Raj'i
C) Three divorces
D) Invalid

Key Takeaway
In divorce by simile to a mountain, the mufta bihi Hanafi ruling is ba'in, meaning the marriage ends irrevocably.
